This eminent Specialist, Rounder, and Chief

He has devoted a life- time to the study of
Nervous Complaints.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated; with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.

ECONOMY IN SMALL THINGS.
Premier Sir G. Republican Dibbs, K.C.M.G., has ordered that the N.S.W. Civil Service notepaper is simply to have a crown stamped on the corner, and the
words “Now South Wales” below, in plain, black letters.
N.S.W.: “And while you're about it. Sir George, don’t you think that you and the Civil Service could economise a lot in time and ink by leaving out
those four letters after your name I”

SATURDAY, JANUARY 14, 1893.
The Workman’s Paradise.
According to the latest published accounts,
the “ New Australia” movement stands very
much where it did some two or three mouths
ago. The scouts of the new Pilgrim Blathers
are reported to be still prospecting in the tree-
less wastes in the region of the Rio Negro in
quest of that Paradise which is always just a
little w r ay ahead, and the main body of the
crusade awaits their report before moving.
Perhaps it would have been better had the
Paradise been found a little earlier, before the
movement was quite so far advanced. The
leaders are now too deeply committed to draw
back with any semblance of dignity, and if the
looked-for Eden can't be discovered there will
be a great temptation to select the least for-
bidding chunk of desert available, and take the
risk. In any case, the prospects in southern
Argentina are not brilliant, and the choice is
comparatively restricted. The Promised Land
was the ancient sea-bottom of the Atlantic
before the Argentine was heaved up out of the
waters, and the sand and gravel and shingle
that formed the floor of the old ocean are still
there in boundless profusion. It is mostly a
rainless region—even Australia can present no
such record as that of the western provinces for
cast-iron drought, but by much elaborate
irrigation with the waters of the snow-
river that flows down from the Cordil-
leras the wastes of ’ 4 lSew' Australia” may
possibly be converted into some semblance
of fertility. Also, there are saline swamps here
and there, alleged to be the last remains of the
vanished ocean, and at intervals there are miles
of glaring salt, and the climate ranges from
many degrees below freezing limit to an un-
known quantity in the shade. It is in the
home of all these varied phenomena that the
pioneers of the crusade are now alleged to be
picking their precarious way. When they have
selected the least forbidding portion of this
land of gaunt despair, and got their title-deeds
from the Government—if the Government lasts
long enough to give them, and doesn’t change
its mind, and provided the deeds are of any
practical value after they are procured, and
assuming that the concession isn’t revoked by
some subsequent Government, or revolutionary
committee, or junta, or dictator, and that the
Indians, who put in a loose and general claim
all the Waste lands of the Confederation,
don’t stand off the settlers with clubs and
violence —then the first stages of the work will
be practically complete,
* * »
There is a precarious and stormy element
about Argentine politics which makes the in-
section of tiie above-mentioned saving clauses
eminently advisable. At present there is a
temporary hill in the general tumult and
desolation, but the Id,story of La Plata, ever
since the beginning of its independence, lias
been so wildly riotous, that a continuance of
peace is hardly to be hoped for. The free-
dom of Argentina began with the elec-
tion of President Paykidon in 1816, where-
upon Abtigas at once commenced the
inevitable insurrection, and the Brazilians inter-
fered to suppress him, and Paykidon “went
foi both piii'ties in a wild-cat struggle
which lasted with varying fortunes for ten years.
\\ hen it was over, a new insurrection imme-
diately ensued, and President Dorrego was
captm ed and shot without ceremony. Plis suc-
cessor, Rosas, proceeded to avenge his death,
and hung all sorts and Conditions of men pro-
miscuously for ten years, till the French inter-
vened. A fresh insurrection arose after their
departure, but was promptly suppressed, and
the leader hanged or otherwise disposed of.
ihen Rosas raged for twelve years more, and
slaughtered, with or without legal formality,
night and day ; he became a shrieking maniac in
Ins old age, and all the land was filled with blood
and murder and bones and sudden death. The
British endeavoured to overset him, but failed ;
and, finally, the Brazilians poured down on the
distracted country and drove the aged horror
into exile. Urquiza followed him, and Alsina
started the usual insurrection; Lagos com-
menced a second insurrection and suppressed
Alsina. and Pacheco and Pintos started a
third rebellion ; and, finally, all the assorted
rebellions met in one grand crash, and the
Argentine burst up into an assortment of ruins.
These ruins fought with each other madly and
promiscuously for ten years more until the
armies on all sides were nearly exterminated,
and then the Confederation re-united and began
afresh. °
* * • *
Naturally, the first proceeding was to start a
new war, and Argentina and Brazil entered
upon a hideous six years’ conflict with Para-
guay. It is the only war of modern times in
which the whole male population of a country
was practically extinguished ; Paraguay is now
a nation of women and ruins and ghastly
memories, and men are almost as soaree as
potatoes on a gallows. During this struggle
the usual insurrection started in Argentina,
but after a gory campaign it was suppressed.
Another insurrection started as soon as the
former onewas finished, and raged for two
years till 18/3, when it was also extinguished
in blood and ashes and general destruction.
1 he insurrection of 1874 commenced before it
was ripe, and was wiped out in two gory
battles. And since then, except for two more
insurrections, an occasional disagreement with
Chili, a general bankruptcy and the utter
smash of almost every financial institution in the
country, one pestilence, a debt of 11170,000,000,
a species of military paralysis, consequent on the
destruction of much of the native population in
many wars and revolutions, which enables the
Indians to raid the outlying portions of the
country almost unchecked, and a general dis-
satisfaction over the conscription, Argentina
has been, comparatively speaking, at peace
* * *
Most of these insurrections were fought out
in the Eastern and Central provinces, and while
they were in progress the inhabitants of the
outlying regions scrambled, every man for him-
self. Indians, half-breeds, cowboys, and mis-
cellaneous frontier bullies took advantage of
the troubles to set up miniature republics or
dictatorships all over the country, and after a
brief and stormy reign these microscopic adven-
turers either escaped with the loot or were shot
in a counter-insurrection, A great revolt in
the provinces around the capital and several
hundred smaller revolts of two men and a dog,
scattered throughout the rest of the land, were
for many years the normal conditions of the
Confederation. But except when a dictator of
the Rosas type comes round to hang promiscu-
ously that section of the population which
failed to join him in his last successful insur-
rection, the remote and thinly-peopled regions
of Argentina are practically without any per-
manent or visible form of government. More-
over, there was only one Rosas, and there is
never likely to be another, therefore /in
these parts the Isew Australia emigrants may
be practically a law unto themselves, and if the
chronic revolution which is in the air, and the
cold, and the desolation, and the universal
hopelessness, lead them at last to split up into
factions and pursue each other with guns and
other instruments, there is no visible power to
prevent them doing so. The constitution of
New Australia is apparency built on the
assumption that brotherly love will prevail
among the settlers for ever ; there is nothing in
it about the erection of a gaol or the formation
of a penal code ; and unless hardened offenders
are marooned on the frozen wilds of Patagonia
there seems to be no provision for the mainten-
ance of any discipline whatsoever.
*. * #
The reasons which induced the Argonauts of
Queensland to select this strangely unpromising
land for their experiment seem likely to remain
an eternal mystery. There are attractions
about many of the South Sea Islands which
might induce a weary Australian, tired of
the droughts, the poverty, the Chinamen, tired
of land-grabbing, tired of the ever-increasing
struggle for existence, and of the utter rotten-
ness of a jerry-built civilisation, to attempt
founding an ide.il community somewhere amoim
the coral and the p.alm-groves. There are at
least reasonable prospects in Maorilaud, where
a democratic Government is systematically
bursting up the big estates for the benefit of
agricultural settlers, and where 50,000 acres of
average soil are worth more than 1000 square
miles of shingle and sand and salt and old, dis-
used sea-bottom in Argentina. There is also
land enough still to be obtained in the western
division of New South Wales or Queensland, in
the interior of South Australia, or in the great
western province, which is not one iota more
desolate or more remote from the nearest avail-
able market than the brightest elysium that is
likely to be obtained gratis in Argentina, and
which can be bought for much less than the cost
of the journey to that joyous land of revolution.
Barring the haze of romance that is born of
stupendous distances, there is nothing good in
New Australia which Old Australia or Maori-
land cannot offer in equal profusion. New
Australia and the New Jerusalem owe their
glories to the same cause—their remoteness.
Jhe exodus to the Paradise in Argentina is
merely another instance of the blind credulity
which, years ago, led the British Hodge to
start by hundreds for another Paradise in Utah,
under the domination of Brigham Young—
which made the Scotch go forth in thousands
to colonise the fever-stricken, death-infested,
ghost - haunted swamps of Panama which
created the great emigration movements to
Brazil, to Palestine, to Uruguay, to Greenland
of all lands beneath the sun, and to any
number of other regions equally ill-fated. But
the present movement has less excuse than any
of them. Emigration is played out. There was a
time, years ago, when there was still some sem-
blance of a happy land ahead for the emigrant
who kept on “going West,” but that resource is
exhausted. There is no longer any West. The
West has brought up with a crash against the
East—the newest civilisation and the oldest
have met—the circle of the earth is complete.
It is no longer of any avail to fly from the old
enemy—Monopoly. There is no place left to
fly to. The only resource that remains is tm
stand and fight. /
The Judicial Lock-Out.
A Sydney barrister had to go ninety miles by
train the other day to find a Judge to grant an
injunction, or put a new spring in a demurrer.
The province pays seven Supreme Court Judges
to attend to the recpiirements of litigants, yet
when a Sydney suitor has a piece of legal busi-
ness to be done in haste, he must pay a barrister
to travel all the way to Sutton Forest. The
Supreme Court shutters have been put up for
the holidays, and will stay up till near the end
of February. Apparently, it takes the Judges
two months to get over Christmas. They have
all gone out of town, and the limpid stream of
justice is turned off at the meter. Time does
not run at law or in equity, and about the
only thing in a legal way that can be done
without difficulty is to commit a contempt of
court. And that, we may mention by the way,
is what the Sydney daily press has been indus-
triously doing in the very matter to which the
Sutton Forest trip related.
* * #
It is hard on Judge Owen that he should
have had to interrupt his holiday and return to
Sydney over a copyright dispute. He has done
a lot of work during the year, and is entitled
to rest. But it is harder on the people in
whose interests the legal machine is ostensibly
run, and who pay for running it, that for about
three months in the year the courts are idle.
True, a litigant during vacation may perhaps
get his wants attended to if he can discover in
what part of the country one of the judges is to
be found, and can afford to pay a lawyer to go
and beard him in his den. (It would be inter-
esting to know how much it cost to bring
Judge Owen down from Sutton Forest). Rut
again, the Judge, even when found, may refuse
to touch the work, no matter how urgent it is.
In fact, such refusals are common. And, if a
Judge does refuse, there is no way of coercing
him. The only thing to be done is to hunt up
another in the hope that lie may prove more
tractable.
* # *
Now, why should the whole community,
during what is called vacation, be put in the
position of humble petitioners to their gracious
majesties the Judges? Why should the
Judges have the power of arbitrarily set-
ting apart a great part of the year as
vacation ? And why should there be any
such thing as vacation at all ? We do
not mean to suggest that the Judges should
be deprived of their holidays. Withouftmore
information on the subject than we possess,
we do not even say that their allowance
of holidays is excessive. It is said, and it
may be true, that some of the occupants of
the N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench spend a
considerable part of vacation in disposing of
arrears of work. However that may be, so
long as the Court sits, they have a good deal to
do, and they have to do it under conditions that
are a standing disgrace to the province. No
man, if he had the constitution of an ostrich,
should be expected to do a day’s work in the
filthy atmosphere of some of the Sydney
courts. A Judge who does work there deserves
a holiday when he gets it. But it is quite
possible to give the Judges their holidays with-
out laying up all the legal machinery of the
country for months at a time. When Railway -
Commissioner Eddy went off to England it
wasn’t found necessary to close the railway-
lines till he came back. We don’t need to burn
down a house to roast a pig.
* * #
There is no reason on earth why the Supreme
Courts of all the provinces should not remain
open all the year round. Except on certain
specified holidays you can go to the Post-office
and buy stamps on any day in the year. And
there should be precisely the same facilities for
getting from the Supreme Court the commodity
on sale there. Why not? There is no more
divinity about a Supreme Court Judge than
about the other Government official who sells
postage-stamps. If a great department like
the Post-office can so arrange its work tlmt the
officials can all get their annual holiday in
rotation, without interfering with the discharge
of their public duties, surely seven Judges have
intelligence enough among them to make a
similar arrangement. If they have not, then
the power of making the arrangements should
be taken out of their hands.
• * •
The law’s delays are bad enough without the
aggravation of vacations. It takes a creditor
several months to recover a simple debt at law
under the most favourable circumstances. There
are cases in New South Wales Supreme Court,
of the most ordinary type, that were com-
menced in ’9l, and are” ~still awaiting final
decision. Some of the more complex even go
back beyond the memory of man. The vaca-
tion nuisance, of course, doesn’t account for
more than a very small proportion of the pre-
posterous and costly delay. The legal system
is so rotten with abuses, that singling out any
particular one for admiration seems a futile
nismess enough; but this happens to be an
abuse that stands isolated, and can be dealt
with on its own merits.
• # *
It may be remarked that, so far as the Judges
themselves are concerned, they do not seem
possessed of any strong desire to get awav
together. They don’t spend their vacation
playing skittles in the same back yard. The
supreme joy of the half-yearly spell, with some
ot them at any rate, must be the chance of
escaping the unwelcome propinquity imposed
jy sittings in Banco. When we see Judge
n.ANk and Judge Dash walking down the
s ieet arm-in-arm we shall commence to look
out for a comfortable place to spend the millen-
nium. So that, if the Judges’ inclinations are
to be regarded, the rotatory holiday would
probably lie a decided advantage. It would
enable Ins Honor to go fishing without having
to post his tipstaff on an adjacent rock to give
warning of the approach of any invading bar-
rister with a hungry glare in his eye and a brief
under his arm,
* « «
Not that from The Bulletin’s point of .view-
it matters whether the Judges like the change
or , , The Supreme Court has not been-
es ablished for the benefit of the men who sit
on the Bench. Their business is to do the work
t ley are paid to do, and to do it in the wav
that meets best the interests of the public who
pay them. If they are not paid enough for
doing it in that way, give them more. If they
are overworked, appoint another Judge, or a
couple, or half-a-dozen more. If they are
incapable of doing it, or won’t, get someone
who will. However it is managed, the Court
ought to be open for the despatch of business,
except on bank holidays, day in and day out all
through the year.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Irish Outrage.
Tins is the season when the Horrible Fenian
Outrage is in full bloom in Ireland, and the
dynamiter is abroad. Until the recent bomb-
explosion at Dublin, there was just one homi-
cide in that distressful land during nearly
twelve months, and the Tory press promptly
cabled it all over the earth' with the garish
heading, “Terrible Agrarian Murder inlreland!”
As it chanced, it proved to be only a case where
a policeman, employed in keeping up the Tory
regime and enforcing the Coercion Act, went
mad and ran amok, but though this was duly
explained afterwards (in small type), the great
majority of the community, whose ideas are
always hazy, only remembers the incident
as another proof that the brutal Celt wants to
be kept down with an iron hand. In Fmgland,
during same twelve months, no less
than 150 murderers were brought to trial
and more than 200 murders were committed,
yet there was no Coercion Act, no frenzied out-
cry that the English nation wanted to be Kept
Down by martial law, no constant eruption of
cablegrams about the horrible outrage in
England—nothing. The English and Scotch,
as demonstrated by their own criminal statistics,
are, and have been for 30 years, the most
criminal, the Irish the least criminal, section of
the British community, and the gap between
the two races keeps steadily widening. Yet
the instant a Dublin policeman is killed—
though there is absolutely no evidence to show
by whom—that sickening hypocrite, the Bull
at the other end of the electric cable,
sends forth the old, old story about the “ Hor-
rible Outrage in Ireland.” And, meanwhile,
the horrible outrage in England only arrives
slowly by post, and when the blood-spattered
English paper arrives with its long list of sordid
horrors, no one seems to take any special notice
of it. I here is a murder in almost every
column of the English weekly—some hideous
story of trampled brains and caved-in bones
and squashed flesh and blood—and yet it all
passes comparatively unnoticed. The Austra-
lian editor is too busy faking up his leading
article about the new “outbreak” in Ireland
that he has no time to notice such trifles.
Glassey's Programme.
Ihe following is alleged to be the programme
on which the Queensland Labour-party intends
to contest the coming general election ;
An Australian Republic, State ownership of the mines
a minimum wage of £3 per week for miners, with a maxi-
mum of 44 hours’ work, a land tax, increase of the pas-
toral rents, State-aided settlement, substitution of
volunteers for the present naval and military system
radical changes in the Railway Department 'and Civil
Service, &c., and the liberation of the strike prisoners
who shall be compensated in part by money deducted
from the salary of the Judge who sentenced them.
The above programme is supposed to have been
drafted by Mr. Glassey, and The Bulletin
hereby warns that gentleman that he is taking
about the shortest possible road to the final
wreckage of the Labour-party and all its pros-
pects. In the first place, the strenuous efforts
to raise the wages and shorten the working
hours of the miners only, while other toilers
whose hours are far longer and whose wages are
much lower are altogether neglected, looks
so like an unscrupulous pandering to that
section of labour which can give a big, solid,
commanding vote, and a brutal disregard of the
sections which can’t do so, that it will excite
general disgust. The all-round recognition of the
eight hours principle is one of the first objects of
the Australian Democracy, yet vast numbers of
workers, from the ’bus-driver down to the poor

still toiling from 10 to 18 hours per diem,

neglected, presumably because their suffrages
are not an appreciable influence at a general

who has already got down as nearly as

than 7J. Also, the neglected 14-hour man is
expected to put his whole soul into the work
of securing this reduction, and if he doesn’t he
is a traitor to the cause. The fixing of miners’

The best part of His-tory is Her-story.
There is said to be in London an old maid
who has taken only milk with her tea since the
trial of Dr. Thomas Neill Cream.
Angry freetrader stops his subscription to
Cobargo Watch (N.S.W.): “I am not going to
pay any man £1 a year to call me a liar.”
The British Channel Tunnel scheme is
revived. All hands agree it is feasible, the design
having emanated from Thome de Gamond, French
engineer, 60 years ago. The tunnel can be put
through the chalk for eight millions sterling, while
the Channel Bridge would cost, probably, 20
millions.
said by the way, about any legal minimum for
the other workers, the ones whose votes are so
few as to be hardly worth having, or the
females who have no votes at all—is not a
remedy for any existing evil ; what is wanted
is co-operation, so that the miners shall seeme
the profits which they themselves produce,
whether these profits are £4O a week or ss.
The release of the unionist prisoners is a strictly
reasonable demand, but the idea of compen-
sating them out of the Judge’s salary is a paltry
and pettifogging revenge, utterly unworthy of
sensible men. No such democrats as Grey
or Higinbotham would have proposed to waste
time and energy over so miserable a figment
of personal malice. The urgent necessity for a
big-brained Democratic leader was never so
plainly demonstrated before; the party whose
programme, so far from attracting converts, is
calculated to repel half its old-time adherents,
is in a bad way.
Wanted.
What do we need in our midst to-day,
In this world of fraud and cant—
No matter the part or the game we play—
Say, what do <ve chiefly want ?
Not fight and skite, and the false delight
Of winning a gilded fame,
’But an eye to choose ’twixt wrong and right
And a moderate sense of shame.
In the merchants’ mart and the social dance
One hears the accursed tale—
■“ We should do the same (if we got the chance) ”
As the robber who cheats a gaol.
'The lesson that probity proves the fool,
And trickery takes no blame,
Is dinned in the ears of lads from school
Till it deadens their sense of shame.
'Theibankrupt’s cheek would scorn to blush
For the schedule he gaily files;
No coin has he, but his wife is “ flush,”
And sassiety simply smiles.
The Minister tells with official tongue
His lies so absurdly lame ;
For a rooster perched on his heap of dung
Has a livelier sense of shame.
Once white was white and black was black,
Our grandmothers used to say ;
Now white is “green” in our world, alack !
And black, at its worst, is gray.
The wise are rogues, and men are cranks
Who work for an honoured name,
Whilst the gamblers break their country’s banks
With no shadow of sense of shame.
'Great Heaven, supply the pressing need
Of a land where Corruption’s king,
■ And give us a new and a cleaner creed,
And a song of new hope to sing - ;
Breathe on poor Honesty’s feeble spark,
And fan it again to flame,
And teach us to turn from the way that’s dark
With a moderate sense of shame.
Melbourne.
E.F.
A Voice from Queensland.
‘“Tom Farmer” writes to The Bulletin as
follows:
Your leading article, “The Kanaka Paradise. By Bull's
'Daughter,” in your last issue, is a most unfair and un-
'truthful production. What does the writer of that
article know of Kanakas more than Times’ ; has he ever
seen a Kanaka? Has he ever been on a plantation ? Has
! heever been on a labour vessel? Can he speak any of
the native dialects? It strikes me that the writer of this
(piece of “ bosh ” is a person who writes for his “screw,”
'not for the good of mankind. Those are the sort of
'articles that destroy your paper’s usefulness. No matter
ffiow clever you may write, unless you are writing the
•truth its of no account. Kindly send author of articles
'round -sugar plantations, give him a holiday to
'visit 'the 'districts, let him visit every sugar
iplantation iin (Queensland, not the show ones only.
Let 'him €toy ion 'the worst-conducted ones he finds, and
Uhen'lat him wrlte the truth, the whole truth, and, mark,
JNothino hut the truth. We are all Democrats in these
(colonies, but when your paper descends to print (without
Vcnowing, perhaps) the most bare-faced lies against a large
section of Queensland’s best men, then we feel inclined to
say, no more Bulletin. I am a man who has to work for
his living, but I reckon I know something of the Kanaka
trade, and I tell you your article is rot.
Bulletin hereby invites this foaming
(Correspondent to write again and explain
Whici) statement in jthe article alluded to
|he wishes jtp deny. So far he has merely
ehriekej large, without specifically refut-
ing one single assertion. With regard to his
questions they are easily answered. One of the
writers—for this particular article happened to
be really a joint production—who has so aroused
Mr. Farmer’s bile has seen some hundreds of
Kanakas, and has been on many plantations.
The other writer was for a long while in the
employment of a Queensland firm which owned
ships and imported Kanakas in the good old
days. He received the loud-mouthed hue?
cancers when they returned from a cruise, and
sold firearms to them—they were always craving
for lots of cartridges before they started on a
new voyage ; and he lived at the same hotel
with them and heard their stories when they
were drunk and also when they were sober ;
and he resided with and had a wide and inti-
mate acquaintance among those rum-soaked
hnpersonations of bribery and corruption, the
Government Agents, and was accustomed to
hear them repent and confess their sins when-
ever they were sobering up from the inevitable
shore orgies, and getting ready for sea again.
Likewise he has been to sea in a slaver—from
Queensland to Havannah Harbour—and is still
in constant communication with old friends
long located in the South Seas where the gentle
blaekbirder doth graze. The facts that are not
altogether within the writers’ knowledge, come
from the Queensland mortality returns, from
Griffith’s speeches, from the official leprosy
statistics, and other equally reliable sources.
And just here a few items from the expurgated
official log of the latest slaver (which returned
to Australia last week) are pleasantly instruc-
tive. :
On November 10 Jimmy Bonn, a return, who had been
many years in Queensland, was deliberately shot by his
people shortly after being landed on t lie island of Patna,
and in sight of the boats Whilst recruiting at
Aoba, and with good promise of success, the Mary
Henderson, of Sydney, Captain M'Lkod, a vessel engaged
in copra trading, came into the harbor, and shortly after
wards the natives were in a h-Ipless state of intoxication.
The result was that the Government agent [This illustra-
tion of new-born blackbirding self-abnegation may or may
not be founded on fact, but anyhow the story is purely an
exparte one.—Ed. B.] refused further recruiting. On the
29th the boats were fired at from behind an ambush by
the natives on the weather side of Mallioolo. The place is
a narrow inlet, hemmed i i cn three sides by banks, and
as the boats were unde, way they were subjected to a
cross fire and a raking fire from astern. The boat’s crew
returned the fire, but were unable to ascertain with what
result, as their assailants were in hiding behind boulders
and trees. Fortunately no one in the boats was struck.
The supposition is that the attack was made on account
of a refusal t) supply the natives with rifles. A few days
previously a French cutter had visited the spot and re-
cruited 17 boys, giving in exchange Snider rifles and cart-
ridges, and, as showing how very anxious the natives
were to possess cartridges, the mate of the Foam says
that at Tanna he was offered a £1 note in exchange for
10 of them. Three deaths occurred amongst the returns,
but these were boys who were put on board in a very
sickly condition.
One murder, one free-fight with guns, three
deaths ! How will the report read when the
crew get drunk and start to fill in the pic-
turesque details? And, by-the-bye, when Mr.
Farmer writes again, will he kindly explain
what he knows about the business, for, so far,
he doesn’t even show that he knows anything?

The late George Higinbotham’s desire for a
private funeral sprang, doubtless, from his con-
tempt for the class of men who would inevit-
ably “ represent ” Victoria at the public
obsequies of a Chief Justice. The idea of being
followed to the grave by a gang of blatant
politicians, and lowered to his last resting-place
in the presence of disreputable officers of the
State, was so repugnant to the honest lawyer
that he imposed upon his family the duty of
burying him in the most simple and secret
manner possible. Prosaic admirers of the dead
patriot’s life and works can easily imagine
that he may sleep the better for not having had
his pall borne, or his coffin sighed over, by the
creatures who would fain have “ starred ” in
the valedictory scene. And all who possess
any sense of the eternal fitness of things will
approve of the arrangement which prevented
the corrupt fraternity of leading Victorians,
commonly known as the Friends of Fraud, from
figuring in the press as friends of the incor-
ruptible George Higinbotham.
It is irritating to find newspaper drivellers
coupling the names of Higinbotham and General
Gordon. “In many aspects of his character
the late Chief Justice resembled the Hero of
Khartoum,” &c,, &c., bleats the conventional
ass who totters into print whenever a famous
man dies. This kind of fiction gets repeated
until it passes muster as a fact, and yet the
difference between Higinbotham and Gordon
was about as wide as human nature would allow
it to be. The one was a clear-headed Judge
who wouldn’t willingly hurt a fly, and never
obtruded his piety into public life. The other
was a cranky soldier of fortune, who went
about with a Bible in one hand and a sword in
the other, butchering Chinamen and black-
fellows in a chronic state of religious mania.
Gordon’s professed desire to serve his country
never fitted in with his fighting for the Emperor
of China, anyway. Leaving that incident of
his wild career out of the question, the simple
distinction between lunacy and sanity separates
the mental characteristics of the two men. The
proposal to honour Judge Higinbotham’s
memory with a statue “similar to General
Gordon’s,” reminds us that the monument of
Melbourne’s hysteria ought to be carted away,
and a figure of the real hero erected in its
place.
The Australian Record, a church paper,
rises indignantly to refute Labouciiere’s asser-
tion that the Anglican Bishop of Sydney,
Bishop S. Smith, the Cabmen’s Benefactor, is
overpaid with a salary of £4OOO per annum.
It sets about the job in a woefully clumsy
fashion, and does a lot of damage. For
instance :
The stipend of the Primate is a little over £3OOO a-year.
Considering'the sums gained by the heads of other pro-
fessions, this amount is not at all too much.
According to this the Primate’s job is only a
“profession he “professes ” to have faith in
a glorious resurrection and all the rest of it,
but it is a mere matter of business, and he
must be paid as much for doing it as be could
make in any common, sinful, soul-destroying
trade, or else he’ll throw it up ! It is greatly a
question of cash ; the bright and shining pros-
pect of the New Jerusalem doesn’t count for
much in the calculation, and he hasn’t enough
faith to abate one solitary coin for the harp
and the crown and the rest of the golden
future. Bishop Smith has had several mis-
fortunes in Australia, but his defence by the
Australian Record is the worst of them all.
Melbourne Evening Standard boils over
about the depravity of a Townsville (Q.) citizen,
who told Premier Griffith that he “ had en-
riched himself at the expense of the country ”:
It is hardly necessary to go far back into the pages of
history to ascertain how effrontery of this kind would
have been dealt with in days of yore. For lesser gibes
and affronts men have been sent to the torture-chamber
and the gibbet, and even in latter days it is questionable
whether in either free England or republican France such
freedom of speech to the head of a Government would
pass with impunity. The Londoner who would take it
upon himself at any other than election time to so address
Mr. Gladstone, would probably make the acquaintance
of Bow-street. In Paris, the gendarmes would make very
short work of any individual who thus insulted M. Sadi
Carnot.
The Standard, in its own halting, unfinished
fashion, seems to have a yearning for the return
of these “ days of yore ” when any man who
told an unpleasant truth to the boss slave-
importer of Queensland would have been taken
away and officially skinned. It hasn’t the
courage to say so, but it yearns all the same. It
is interesting to hear the evening paper whoop
over the blessed privileges of free speech, and
at the same time to hear it yell for the thumb-
screw and the boot whenever the privilege is
exercised in a way that doesn’t suit the cast-
iron prejudice of the Contact-with-Leprosy
gang. Yet why its soul should be so harried over
Griffith it is difficult to imagine. Griffith
declared Premier MTlwratth to be a man who
robbed the State and put tho money in his
pocket, and the Standard never demanded
that he should be hanged. Lowry, of Towns-
ville, simply stated Unit Griffith “had en-
riched himself at the expanse of the country,”
which was a mild way of putting it compared
witli the infuriated but specific roar of Samuel
in the ancient times, and the Standard wants
him flayed at once.
In an Orange meeting, at the Ulster Hall,
Belfast, Francis Abigail promised that the Aus-
tralasian organisation would stand by the Irish
body when it “lined the ditches” after Home
Rule. Now, London Star wants to know how
Francis will contrive to answer the bugle-call.
Johnson, M.P.—once a poor pressman in
Adelaide, but now, as he does not forget to inform
acquaintances, the owner of eighty houses —gave
a Christmas treat to 200 children of his tenants.
Tho’ a member of the bogus National Defence
League, Johnson still retains some of his Radical
ideas, and he has made it public that “ he would
not accept a knighthood if offered to him.” We
have heard that before.
last words were, “I have
opened the book ! ” The meaning of the remark
was not exactly clear to the bystanders, however,
and nobody had the commonplace curiosity to ask
which book he referred to. A cynical writer states
that the incident reminds him of a dying friend
who said, “I shall soon be there.” A feeling of
delicacy restrained the company from enquiring
about the route.
New Year “honours” are scarce. Five
disgusted people are W. A. Zeal, President of
Victorian Legislative Council, N. Fitzgerald, of
the same House, Thomas Bent, Speaker of Vic-
torian Assembly, Jenkin Coles, the S.A. Speaker,

There will probably be several Australian knights
in gaol shortly. Pending oakum contingencies,
the Colonial Office will be very careful in ladling
out its marmalade.
The new Victorian Chief Justice Madden
wears a plain gold ring bearing a name and a
date. He once dreamt he saw the Melbourne
Cup run, and that a certain number was the
winner. The race came off a day or two later,
and the doctor backed the v\ inning number of his
dream, and advised his frie ids to do likewise.
The dream was verified, and a grateful friend
presented Dr. Madden with the first ring he ever
wore.
Judge Windeyer’s confession to Lady
Sherbrooke that he “entirely” owed his success
to Bob Lowe’s “ great kindness ” to him as a boy,
is stamped with base ingratitude towards bis
bountiful country, N.S.W., which ran a Uni-
versity for his sole benefit in his teens, and billeted
him in political and judicial offices for the rest of
his life. Here we recall Macleay’s historic jibe,
when young Windeyer was as yet the only gra-
duate of the young Sydney University: “We
have spent =£60,000 on a University, and that ”
pointing to Windeyer—“is what we have got for
it.”
A memorial tablet in Derry Cathedral
testifies to the dutiful life of Judge Higinbotham’s
brother Robert, who died some years ago. Robert
Higinbotham was the clergyman of the family,
known to Australia only by his epitaph ;
Down through our crowded lanes and closer streets,
O friend, how beautiful thy footsteps were ;
When through the fever’s waves of fire they trod,
A form was with thee like the son of God ;
’Twas hut one step for those victorious feet
From their day’s walk into the golden street;
And they who watched that walk so bright and brief
Have marked this marble with their hope and grief.
The late Chief Justice Higinbotham (writes
a Bulletin correspondent) was a “ tigerish ”
worker. He once said he would sit on Sunday if
necessary, and he used to infuriate the Bar by
sitting late on Saturday. When Clarence Whistler,
the wrestler, made his first appearance in Mel-
bourne on a Saturday, the industrious Chief Jus-
tice, who happened to be hearing a big ease in
which Dr. Madden was appearing, said he would
sit till four o’clock. “Surely, your Honor,” ex-
postulated Dr. Madden, “you would not deprive
us of seeing the great Mr. Whistler !” The Court
adjourned early.
“Demos”: “I only had the honour of
speaking to Higinbotham once, and it was whilst
reporting the inquest on his brother, the railway-
engineer, in his Brighton house—Tom Higin-
botham was a bachelor, and died suddenly even
as his great brother has just done. I said to the
Chief Justice, ‘I have been reading over your
speeches of the past, in Hansard, with delight.’
He answered, ‘You could not do anything more
unprofitable.’ Then I said, ‘Liberalism is beaten.’
He replied, ‘ I am sorry to hear any man say so,
and would be sorry to believe it.’ I asked him to
show me his library and he pointed to a small
book-case, containing only about 50 books.
J. J. Casey might have had the ghost of a
show for the Victorian Chief Justiceship by stick-
ing to polities. The once aggressive little pot of
grease has long subsided into the County Court
justiciary, and has only popped his head up once
since he got there, when he had the front to
inform manager Musgrove, on a public occasion,
as to where the handsomest ballet could be got—
viz., at Frankfort. Muzzy looked like a New-
foundland dog at a pug poodle, or like Middle-
wick, the Butterman, in “ Our Boys,” when Sir
Talbot Champneys ventures to contradict him in
the assertion that the pat of butter before them is
“ Dosset.”
The late police-superintendent Winch, of
Victoria, figured in some celebrated cases in his
time. One of the latest was at Geelong 20 years
ago, where the widow of a police magistrate was
charged with falsely registering a child as that of
her late husband. She was discharged ai the
Police Court. Another was the murder of an
engineer, named Reynolds, at his office in Queen-
street, Melb., by a box of explosives. Reynolds,
on arriving one morning 17 or 18 years ago, found
a small box, addressed to him, lying on the window-
sill next his office door. When lie picked it up it
at onoe exploded and killed him. Winch got
possession of the fragments of the box and
attempted to prove that the address was in the
handwriting of one Ruperti Green, an American,
who was arrested and tried two or three times
but ultimately acquitted.
Australasian politics are in a tumbled
condition. In N.S. Wales, the Dihbs Govt,
is only left in power by reason of the divided con-
dition of the third party. In Victoria, the Shiels
party has retained office because of the fear that
any possible set of successors might be twice as
timid and three times as justice-blind. In
Queensland, chaos and a guerilla scramble, with
an election in the foreground. In S. Australia, a
Downer scratch Government that from its birth
avowed itself as only a stopgap until the
pending elections. In Maoriland, a Premier
afflicted with a sickness of which a give-and-take
mystery is made. In Tasmania, even, where
politics are a neutral-tinted trade for spending
public money, the chances are evens as to the result
of any big division. In W. Australia, a Tory
Government which has formulated a land-gift
immigration scheme that is intended to stop with
gold-fillings the hollow teeth of absentee syndi-
cates and Conservative capitalists.

SUNDRY SHOWS.

Opera House “Fritz.”
Some time last week a violent compromise
broke out between Miss Clara Merivale and
George Rignold, and the iron hoof of oppression
having been removed the former commenced her
season at Sydney Royal on Saturday night with
“Falka.” The Royal, in its new guise, looks
better than it ever did before, and a vigorous
effort has evidently been made to place it on a
level with the magnificence of the Lyceum, and
to extinguish all smaller rivals. It is decorated in
non-assertive colours, and through the absence of
glaring hues it looks cool and refreshing, like a
great shandygaff in a thirsty land. The drop-
scene represents a range of splintered blue moun-
tains stacked up against the clouds, a Chinese city
slumbering beside a placid harbour, and in the
foreground, outside the bar, a junk and two sam-
pans labouring furiously in a tempestuous sea
with the pig-tails of the crew standing out
horizontally against the sky. The management
has done its best to make “ Falka ” worthy of its
surroundings, and has met with a fair amount of
success. The opera is constructed out of rather
scanty materials, being mostly based on a girl who
is disguised as a boy, a boy who is disguised as a
girl, a young nobleman disguised as a waiter, a
Hungarian magnate disguised in drink, a huge,
greasy, gluttonous monk who is always burying
his inflamed countenance in victuals and drink, a
bandit and his sister, and a chorus. And the
troubles of these people arise out of the fact that
the young nobleman has been summoned from a
humble billet as a school teacher to assume a lofty
station, but has to pass himself off as a flunkey in
his own ancestral castle because he has committed
a breach of promise with the bandit’s sister, and
is afraid of being carved up; while his sister, who
has eloped from a convent, personates him
and orders him about ; and his sister’s lover
is captured by the drinky monk in her stead, and
compelled to get into a misfit skirt and go back
with him to the nunnery, where he rises in revolt
and scares seven years growth out of an eminently
respectable ladies’ seminary.
• # #
There are patches of really beautiful music
in the show, and the aggregate brain-power of the
company is considerable. Miss Clara Merivale is
an attractive Falka, especially in military uniform;
her song “ ’Tis Eventide,” in particular, is a very
fine performance, and throughout she makes Falka
a sweet and kissable school-girl of the olden
time. All the same, she is a lamentably bad
shot with a property vegetable, and can’t hit
a disguised nobleman at two yards range.
She certainly should roll the tomato in-
stead of throwing it. Miss Josephine Deakin acts
at least equally well, and her song, “There
was no ray of light,” rendered in a clear and
liquid voice, is very artistic. This pretty little
lady has the blessed advantage of always being
audible, whereas the a verage songstress might sing
a price-lut of agricultural implements without
anyone being the wiser. A certain Arthur Hunter,
alleged to be recently imported from England,
perpetrates the huge burlesque brigand Boleslaa,
and is one of the two really funny men of the
company (another individual mistakes himself for
the other funny man, but that is his own error).
He has a melodious voice and a murderous
arsenal of weapons. Brother Pelican, played
by Henry Hodson, is the other funny man, and
is obviously stuffed with a spring mattress. Mr.
C. Leumane is the man who supposes himself to
be wildly comic, wherein he suffers from a delu-
sion, being only an over-acted burlesque; and
Messrs. Allen and Leopold and Miss Ada Conroy
fill various orifices, where the cold blast of
criticism might otherwise whistle through and
afflict the opera with the rheumatism of public
disapprobation. The scenery is excellent, and a
young lady who does nothing in particular bills
herself as “Miss Lilly Langtry.”
#♦ » *
Sydney Garrick, which hasn’t been pros-
pering of late, closed up on Monday night, under
depressing circumstances. Nobody ever quite
knew how sad “ Pink Dominoes ” could be made
to appear until Fred B. Norton’s company pro-
duced it in all its profound solemnity, and his com-
pany marched heavily around, and hid under tables,
and concealed themselves in side-rooms, with a
cold, business-like air which suggested that they
were the latter-end of a ten-days walking-match.
The advertisement was the only thing which kept
up its pecker to the bitter end ; when the show
broke up, the newspapers had the announcement
of a “glorious, surprising, and hilarious success,”
still standing. This sort of thing has been done
so often, however, that the bluff has ceased to pay
dividends. If some manager who is running a
downhill show would have sufficient originality
to advertise that his house is the emptiest one
in the city, and that the takings up to date
haven’t paid the gas-bill, The Bulletin firmly
believes that the refreshing truthfulness of the
statement would bring a crowd along immediately.
** * *
Wirth’s Circus, a strong combination which
appeals eloquently to a horse-loving community,
still holds possession of York-street rink, Sydney,
and gets liberal support. Governor Jersey lends
his countenance to the show on Monday, 16th
inst. The management is understood to have
made a liberal offer to Premier Dibhs to induce
him to prohibit something and afterwards with-
draw the prohibition under extreme pressure, but
that flinty politician refuses to do business.
* * * *-
“Tommy” Hudson, of the well-known
“ Surprise Party ” —whoso last appearance in
Sydney was at the dead-and-gone Queen’s Theatre,
eleven years ago—commences a two weeks’ season
at the Garrick on 28th January.
** * *
Other Sydney entertainments, consisting
of two pantomimes, one Shakespearean comedy,
anothercircus, twoaquariums, one Harry Rickards,
and a varied assortment of mixed nigger minstrel
exhibitions present very few new features. In the
struggle between the two pantomimes, the Lyceum
is getting distinctly ahead, the melancholy of the
rival shriek having begun to eat into the public soul.
The Shakespearean show at the Criterion is doing a
business which is calculated to make the ancient
dramatist turn over in his grave as he reflects that
his copyright, if he ever had one, ran out more
than two centuries ago, and his ghost can’t collect
the royalties. Meston’s corrobboree has been
stopped at the School of Arts, the howls of his
untamed savages having scared the lady-sub-
scribers and led them to imagine that thecompany
was about to break loose, therefore the natives
now howl at the Bondi Aquarium only. Also,
Mr. Meston wishes it un-
derstood that it was not he
who delivered an inaudible
address and wore his pants
turned up at the feet at
Bondi, as asserted last
week by “ Sappho.” That
lady having gone to Tas-
mania for a holiday her
letter doesn’t appear this
week, and the dramatic
critic apologises on her
account.
*» * *
We give herewith a
portrait of Mile. Schera-
zade (Mrs. Russell), the
fascinating lion-tamer of
Fillis’ Circus, who has
added to her reputation as
a subduer of wild beasts
by taming the present
N.S.W. Premier. The re-
doubtable Sir Dibbs, ap-
parently on the represen-
tations of a Ministerial
colleague of his who had
taken to the circus a lady
who was appalled to faint-
ing-pitch by the daring
exhibition of the Schera-
zade, issued an order pro-
hibiting the performance.
However, the interdict did
not last long, for Madame,
having induced the Premier
to attend a private exhibi-
tion at the circus, con-
vinced him (so he says—a
man must have some excuse
for turning himself inside
out) that the danger of the
performance had been
greatly overstated. Mile.
Scherazade is an excellent
type of the class of woman
whose tact and resolution
have done so much for her
native country, France.
She is the wife of Captain
Russell, who shares the
dangers of the lion’s den,
and is altogether so charm-
ing a woman that it says
very little for the good
taste of her pets that
they haven’t eaten her
long ago. She has been
bitten on several occasions ; once she had her
arm badly mauled, and once a lion bit her right
through the breast, but on each occasion she
struck back, got the beast in order, and finished
the performance. She this week takes a most suc-
cessful and enthusiastic benefit, at which her new
lion, Dibbs, has promised to be present.
** * *
The prospectus of the “Prison Hulk
* Success Company, Limited,” which is published
this week, is a document containing some steep
and alarming statements. It says nothing about
the fact that the aged ruin sank in dead calm
water at such short notice that the caretaker had
only time to seize his clothes and fly for his life.
But it sets forth that the lately-submerged wreck
is “as strong as wood and iron can make her,”
is double-planked, two feet thick below the water-
line—if she were two miles thick that mysterious
sinkage still remains unaccounted for, and that
“she made no water through the bilge” on her
passage from Sydney to Melbourne. “Competent
marine experts have pronounced her perfectly safe, ”
and thecompany feelscertain thatshocan beinsured
at a very small advance on current rates ; also, a
well-known firm is willing to load her with a
cargo of wool for Chicago. What time the firm
expects her to arrive for the wool-sales isn’t
specified, but The Bulletin is of opinion that an
ocean race should be organised, the competitors to
be the Success, the Wolverene, the Vernon, the
old Manly steamer Emu, one dredge, anv Balmain
ferry-boat which happens not to be sunk, and a
certain green - and - mud - coloured craft which
coughs wearily to Pynnont and back, fare one
penny. The Bulletin hereby puts its little six-
pence on the green-and-mud-coloured boat from
Pynnont, with the Emu for a place. A com-
petent captain—Vanderdecken, we presume—has
offered his services. When the Success gets off
Cape Horn, Vanderdecken will probably get wet.
The course of pantomime runs smoother
than it did, at Melbourne Princess’s, and one
doesn’t hear of damsels weeping at the wings or
going on to dance with their hearts bowed down.
It is time, however, that the whole company
became profoundly jealous of Ali Baba’s donkey,
because the fun of the show seems to condense
itself more and more into the dumb animal afore-
said. Up goes the donkey in estimation. This
old friend, said to be the “property man” in
private life, has played the same part in former
pantos., and his performance is now so thoroughly
artistic and enjoyable, especially about the back
and near hind leg, that one begins to understand
why the ordinary actor is not a man of property.
It’s on account of him not being comic enough.
The incident likewise suggests that a good many
comedians ought to swap billets with a scene-
shifter, or give the call-boy a chance. Lonnen
works harder than ever for his Ali-money, and
shines in the reflected light of a diminutive boy
whom he has taught to sing “I’vechucked up the
push for the donah ” in much the same way
as a small sparrow chirps like its mother. This
item is making itself felt in many domestic circles
of Melbourne, where the white-haired darling of
the family plays at chewing a quid of tobacco
while he pipes his awful lay to the underfed
governess, or cocks his innocent little eye at the
lady-help. Snazelle still ruminates in the back-
ground without getting a bite at a topical song,
“BULLETIN" ENG. CO, HYDEZT.
MADAME JASIA SCHERAZADE (Li[?] Tamer).
and none of the Forty Thieves appear to be down
with the influenza. Neither is there any osten-
sible reason for these marauders forming them-
selves into the tail of a dragon that blows
fireworks from its nose, but they do this deed
religiously, notwithstanding. For the rest, the
scenery and magnificence are always there when
wanted, but the general effect leaves an aching
void in the intellect which only another Billee
Barlow could adequately fill up.
«« * «
At the Royal, a philanthropic management
has had its “attention directed to the general
depression of the times,” and has recognised the
same in a feeling manner by selling "parcels of
six tickets at reduced prices. ' Meanwhile, “The
Babes in the Wood ” keeps pace with the course
of events in the matter of localising. Somebody
throws off a seasonable joke every now and then,
and Clias. Fanning, as an assassin with an acces-
sible mouth, roars with some humour. Bella
Bashall appears to have placated the limelight
man, and the clown is happily despatched as
before. The kings and queens of England
don’t improve on acquaintance. Henry the Eighth
isn’t good enough to take the chair at a Mormon
tea fight, and any growing lad could knock out
Cneur de Lion in four rounds. In addition to
which the Reigning Sovereign has acquired a mild
stare that doesn’t harmonise with her escort of
Life Guards on ponies, and a sham warrior on a
reduced copy of a horse is but a poor thing, any-
way. On the other hand, Brunton’s transforma-
tion-scene atones for the early efforts of an infant
on a tight rope, so the pantomime, considered
from all points of view, is doing about as well as
can be expected.
** * *
Melbourne Opera House was recently the
scene of a violent attack upon the manaerement,
but the insurrection has happily subsided, and the
olive-branch waves behind the curtain as a token
of belief that the “ghost ” will walk when he can.
On Saturday night they revived “ Fritz,” wherein
Tom Buckley did his beat to produce an echo of
the late Joe Emmett in the name-part. On the
whole, Buckley might have done worse, for
although he never touched the heart, he managed
to thump a drum with considerable dexterity.
“Fritz,” too, is one of those variety dramas to
which the Opera House company can do “ample
justice.”
*- * * *
The Alexandra fulfilled its destiny by
closing the front door, a few nights since. “Lady
Audley’s Secret ” was the last bill at this estab-
lishment, and after a forlorn attempt to sell the
mystery of her life at a low scale of charges her
ladyship took up the secret and walked.
** * *
Also, the Victoria Hall has ceased to cater
for delighted audiences, and the “ Battle of
Waterloo,” a fixture of many years in Melbourne,
was to move on after Tuesday. The Gaiety and
St. George’s Hall are clutching at the shekels of
the million as heretofore, while the Australian
Natives propose holding a characteristic festival
at the Exhibition on January 26, the anniversary
of Convict Day.
** * *
Loimen is said to have been much irri-
tated and inflamed by a Bulletin paragraph,
which he took to mean that he wasn’t truly sorry
to hear of Fred. Leslie’s death. It was against a
journalistic picture of the comedian’s woe that the
scoff was directed, and the writer herewith
repeats his suggestion that the world is tired of
believing that sad, yet perfectly natural, events
call for exceptional displays of emotion in stage
life. Indeed the world is making up its mind
that an actor’s profession prohibits him from ever
being quite natural, under any circumstances,
when there are witnesses present. And the
Bulletin par was based upon the supposition
that a pressman was at hand in this instance.
Charity forbid that the sorrow of a player for the
death of a friend should be doubted ! Leslie, as
it appears, stood godfather to Lonnen’s first
child, and good fellowship existed between the
two good fellows, their rivalry was conducted in a
generous spirit, &c. Outside stage circles, how-
ever, a male adult is not prone to weep copiously
and publicly over the loss of his child’s godfather,
or to cry hysterically, “It can’t be true!” (vide
Argus report) when he hears of an unhappily
ordinary event that has recently been fore-
shadowed by cable messages in the daily journals.
While the average citizen continues to suffer the
shocks to which humanity is subject in a non-
theatrical manner, The Bulletin will still go on
remarking that theatrical expressions of anguish
are false to human nature, and unworthy of
especial notice by the public Press. If Mr.
Lonnen can detect any flaws in this calm, judicial
reasoning he must have a neye like a navvk.

Fritz is revived, in a sort of way, at Melb. Opera
House. _ A singular genius was Emmet. He seemed like a

The old-time mansion of Lawyer Duerdin, vacated by
Melbourne Garrison Mess, is next door to where G. V.
Brooke lived, but Brooke’s villa has disappeared in a vast
yawning gap made in the Boom era, for foundations, and
it remains like a hideous quarry.
Every third man you meet in Melbourne grabs hold of
you by the arm, and tells you, as the darkest of secrets,
that Alice Lethbridge “carried on” dreadfully because
Mary VVeir danced an effective serpentine-dance on the
first night of the Princess’ panto. The story goes that
the principal dancer wouldn’t have minded if the other
lady had consented to render her performance ineffective
by certain unsatisfactory arrangements of toilette and
limelight. If this story be true, it is a touching instance
of how thoroughly a professional lady has the best
interests of the management at heart.
The London paper which badly wants to know why
Luscombe Searelle was selected to respond for the press
at an Eccentric Club dinner, instead of Haddon Chambers,
who was present, speaks of Luscombe as “ a squat, self-
assertive person, extremely doubtful about his aspirates,
and having the general appearance of a provincial shop-
keeper."
“Surely,” remarks London Figaro, “it would be worth
Haddon Chambers’ while to turn out fewer plays, but to
give them a better chance of permanency.
The “Continental ” open-air concerts have caught on
in Adelaide. The open-air idea means money in (he
summer, when people will not go inside stuffy theatres.
Harry Roberts, travelling with Bentley, in Maoriland,
was presented the other day with a “ tuatara " (lizard),
one of the big lizards extinct everywhere but on some
islets off the Maoriland coast, and sole link with the
monsters of the coal-era. In travelling he accidentally
let it loose in a hotel, and it nearly frightened to death
the landlord, who trod on it in the dark.
Fred. Appleton, with his pretty daughter, Maud, and
her perfectly beauteous husband, Inman, has been con-
ducting a troupe round Victoria. It is not always you
find the Thespian father-in-law so gracious.
Coppinall over. He advertises that, inasmuch as the
school inspectors have reported on the State school-
children being so ignorant of history, all ought to be
laken at once to see his pantomime Procession of the
Kings and Queens of England.
Fred. Leslie died just as some of the London papers
were commencing to say very nasty things about him.
For instance, said the Hawk three weeks before he passed
away:—“A more uncomfortable, unpleasant evening I
have never passed than the one I was obliged to sit out Tat
the Gaiety last Saturday night, under the huge mockery
of burlesque, opera-bouffe, or whatever else it may be
termed, as represented by the curious group over which
Mr. Fred. Leslie holds supreme sway. A more intensely
senseless performance I have never witnessed, and if, by
importing American wheezes and stage tricks, coupled
with jokes which, to say the least, are funereal, Mr.
Leslie-Torr intends to elevate the English stage, the
quicker we all adjourn to the music-hall the better will it
be for the eradication of our British spleen. That Mr.
Leslie is an excellent imitator of musical instruments no
one will gainsay, but that and an oft-repeated ‘ damn ’ on
the stage, supplemented by expressions of ‘ giddy old ox ’
and ‘giddy old mackerel,’ are not exactly the means
whereby roars of laughter might be obtained from an
intelligent audience, unless if be that that particular
audience has happened to have dined well if not wisely,”
etc.

funeral—that of the young daughter of a well-known

although the profession, male and female, crowded up,

stage, just as Triboulet, the jester, in lingo’s play,
brings up his daughter away from the Court.
The mourners included a lot of young girls, her
companions, in the company of the Virgin, at the
Convent, and they were a singular contrast to the
actresses.

Explorer David Lindsay relates how the
camels used in the recent “Elder” expedition
were, during one stage of the explorations, 24 days
Without water. No other quadruped could be
half so serviceable in waterless Australia, and the
sooner white men find out that it doesn’t take an
Afghan to handle a camel, the better.

Chief Justice Diginbotham had a way of
his own for dealing with the precedence difficulty,
the Victorian Speaker claimed to be the first
commoner in the country, and therefore to have
precedence of the Chief Justice at the opening l of
the Centenary Exhibition. Instead of making a
song about it, Higinbotham simply stayed away—
not even telling bis brother Judges, for fear they
should sympathise with him and also stay away,
to the detriment of the show.

son, who was ousted from Madame Melba’s Pres-
byterian Ladies’ College, because of bis liberal
agrarian, and anti-squattocratic views. Now they
say another leading Melbourne preceptor has got
the happy despatch, because Democracy is perilous
to little Tallowfat.

One of the saddest features of “Forty Thieves,” at
Melb. Princess’s, has been a duet between Lonnon and
Katie Barry. A couple of daily pressman, they say, took
compassion on this wretched ditty and volunteered to
“ write it up ” for the star comedian, whereupon the star
comedian graciously accepted the gratuitous verses, and
didn’t take the trouble to learn them. He and Katie
went on singing the original rubbish as before, and the
item continued to fall as fiat as ever. The moial of which
is that competent journalists shouldn’t attempt to
educate professional comic singers in “ snap,” or sense of
humour. Also, they should understand that the purfesh
in Australia, from the managements downward, objects
to the press writing anything beyond slav< ring “ notib s”
for a theatre. Garner was once heard to say that five
newspaper men were anxious to localise a certain Sydney
pantomime, but he didn’t want “any of those d d
fellows to do the work.”
The Bulletin now learns that Sydney Trades and
Labour Council did not guarantee £lOO expenses to Fillis’
Circus on the night of the Broken Hill benefit. Fillis
undertook to hand over everything in excess of actual
working expenses to the Broken Hill relief fund, but the
weather was unfavourable, the T. and L. Council (so it is
alleged) pushed the business inefficiently, and the show
didn’t realise expenses. Therefore, Fillis lost about £4O,
the Council lost £4 on advertising, and the strike fund
drew a blank. Another benefit is being arranged for
Friday.

The Melbourne daily critics have scored at Professor
Marshall Hall’s expense on the subject of the interpola-
tion of two superfluous bars in Beethoven’s C Minor Sym-
phony. The Professor had previously slated Cowen for
(as he alleged) imitating the great composer by cutting
out those same bars ; and Nesbit, of the Age, proves bv a
letter from the latter to his publisher that they should
never have been printed at all and “must come out.”
There is an old chestnut regarding a conscientious Ger-
man trombone-player who spoilt the performance of a
symphony by a loud blast in the middle of a piano pas-
sage for strings only, and who excused himself to his irate
conductor by saying, “ I see he was only a squashed fly,
but he w; s dere, and so I blayed him.” The Professor
would, no doubt, have gone further and explained the
emotional significance” of the note represented by the
“ squashed fly.”

The latest clue to the owner of the Mel-
bourne “severed limbs” consists of a rumour
about an old deceased pauper whose remains are
alleged to have gone astray on their way to the
•cemetery. The deceased was removed from the
Immigrants’ Home with a view that his bones
might be rattled away in the customary 165.-
tuneral, but it is alleged that he was neither buried
nor officially dissected, and unless lie lias been for-
gotten under the counter the world seems to have
lost track of him altogether.
More Freedom of Contract.
The subscribers to Sydney Hospital had better
keep an eye on the retrenchment party in that
institution. Ex-director Shadler writes to
the daily papers expressing his views on the
subject of cutting down expenses. He prides
himself on having formerly succeeded in getting
the resident doctors’ salaries reduced from £250
each to £IOO, and he now proposes that the
salaries shall be abolished altogether. He
says :
That these former salaries were unreasonably high is
patent from the fact that only two of the officers—[out of
four] -on the reduction coming into force—resigned, and
their places were immediately filled by very willing can-
didates.
ihe fact that only fifty per cent, of the staff
refused to submit to the cut in wages, and that
other men took their places, doesn’t prove that
the former wages were too high. It only proves
that there were cheaper doctors to be got. And
the chief end of the Sydney Hospital is not to
get its work done by cheap men. It evidently
suffers enough already from cheap directors.
Ex-director Shadler goes on :
There are no end of young graduates who are only too
glad and desirous to be associated for twelve months as
resident medical officers in a large hospital. The exten-
sive and varied experience gained by them during that
period, in addition to a twelvemonths’ gratuitous adver-
tisement, &o.
And so on. There are no end of young gradu-
ates who would be only too glad and desirous
to see ex-director Shadler break his leg, or
start an aneurism, or commence to grow a
cancer, or develope typhoid, if they stood a
chance to get the job of repairing him. But
they wouldn’t stand the chance. When the
ex-director Shadler kind of man wants a
doctor, he doesn’t trust his damaged carcase to
the sort of practitioner who is glad and desirous
to do work for nothing, for the sake of the
advertisement. Ho budding sawbones is likely
to get an extensive and varied experience at
the expense of a potentate like Shadler.
But if he is ready to work cheap, Shadler is
quite willing to let him break in his ’prentice
hand on the indigent sufferer who is compelled
to seek in a public hospital the medical skill
that the wealthy tradesman, whose letters are
inserted in the papers, can get at his own home.
Another slice :
In England, in some hospitals, the authorities demand
and obtain a premium from these young men : here we
were formerly paying £IOOO a year and free board and
quai ters, and I might also be pardoned for savin»' to
complete their education.
Just so, though it is a little mixed. In England
they do many things that ex-director Shadler
would perhaps like to see acclimatised in
Australia. Meanwhile, The Bulletin hopes
that the man who would tacitly suggest that
the sick in our hospitals should be handed
over to the tender mercies of any “young
graduate ” willing to pay a premium for the
opportunity of gaining an “ extensive and
varied experience ” and “ 12 months advertise-
ment ” at their expense, will be allowed to
remain an ear-director. We don’t want to be
so English as that, you know. We don’t want
that sort of young man in charge of a big hospi-
tal. ihe hospitals are surely not maintained as
stamping-grounds where half-trained striplings
are to slash round till they acquire skill enough
to be worthy to handle the sacred framework
of ex-director Shadler and his tribe. Who is
ex-director Shadler, anyhow ?
A gentleman, “ described as Francis
Frederick Smith (28), a doctor of medicine,”
has had a hit of bad luck. He was fined £l,
the other day, by Stipendiary Magistrate
Delohery, at the Sydney Central, for a breach
of the train by-laws prohibiting getting on or
off the cars while in motion. According to
reliable statistics, there is a daily average of
5,317 persons who jump off and scramble on to
moving trams. Everybody knows it is a
punishable offence ; and everybody knows it is
practically never punished. There are odd
exceptions. But the proportion of prosecutions
to offences is about one to half-a-million. The
prosecutions are supposed to act as a warning
against risking your life by too much hurry.
We don’t know anything about the circum-
stances of Dr. Smith’s case, but we do know
that the only thing the average prosecution is a
warning against is the reprehensible practice of
cheeking a tram-guard. If a man climbs on a
moving tram in defiance of the regulations, and
thus puts himself in the power of the conductor,
he shouldn’t proceed to be uncivil to that
gentleman. If he does, he has himself to blame
if the conductor takes a half-holiday to get level
with him. So, pending further information as
to the relations between Dr. Smith and the
official who prosecuted him, we suspend our
sympathy with the doctor.
There was an “assize ball” at Armidale
(N.S.W.) some years ago, and the Judge was
among the guests. His Honor danced exuberantly,
and it was noticed, curiously, that he invariably
chose the heaviest girls in the room. “ You seem
fond of dancing,” remarked a fair (not fairy) one,
to whom lie had been particularly attentive'
towards the end of the evening. “ Dancing ! Oh,
no, I detest ic,” was the polite replv; “but my
doctor orders me the most violent exercise, and I
never miss a chance like this; and then —there’s
the supper, you know.” The girl who had thus
been unconsciously carrying out a doctor’s pre-
scription, and innocently helping to achieve “the
most violent exercise,” was, of course, absolutely
delighted.
