Chambers’ Journal credits the “ Soudan
Expedition” with having given Australians their
first important lesson in the utility of camels as a
means of transport in exploring work ! This is as
true as that the “Soudan Expedition ” made Aus-
tralia acquainted with the genus goat. Camels
were used in Yorke’s Peninsula, S. A., early in
the forties; the “Artful Dodger,” Coppin, im-
ported some into Melbourne in the fifties ; the
last Burke-Wills expedition used camels in ’CI ;
Howitt’s Relief expedition, a year later, and
John M‘Kinlay’s still later, did ditto; Sir Thomas
Elder imported into South Australia nearly 100
camels, about ’O4 ; and camels have been familiar
beasts of burden in the desert country of Aus-
tralia ever since. Apropos, thus a correspondent:
“As one practically disinterested but knowing
well the requirements of the country between the
Darling and S. A. borders, I congratulate you
upon the terse and concisely-expressed editorial
note following Mr. Dutton’s letter in your issue of
31/12/92. ‘The Afghan must go,’ but the camels
being needed will remain and increase with
demand. White men can drive them, and horses
become accustomed to them as to the railway
which frightens a horse as much as camels when
first seen. The obstacle to overcome is the pre-
judice which must be met by the promoters of any
i nnovation.— Bakblox.
Two hundred Chinese cabinet-makers have
struck work, in Melbourne, on account of a reduc-
tion of wages. The Bulletin wishes them every
just success, partly because it is a good thing that
wages should be kept up, even by a Chinaman,
and partly because it likes to see the “cheap and
reliable ” going on strike at any time. The
advantage that the Mongol enjoys over the Aus-
tralian, in the present case, is that being a China-
man is a protected industry, and being a white
man isn’t. There is a big import duty on China-
men to protect them from the competition of other
heathens, whereas there is no import duty
on white men whatsoever; therefore, when the
Chow goes on strike, it is impossible to
bring in a few hundred yellow men from
China to take his place. Just here it occurs to us
that, considering the amazing things that can be
done under Australian law, it might be worth
while, when the next stack of “assisted ” paupers
arrives in any Australian province, to get a legal
decision as to whether the white man isn’t a
Chinaman “within the meaning of the Act,” and
liable to poll-tax. The civil code is so utterly
irresponsible a thing, that there is absolutely no
certainty, until the matter is laid before the Full
Court, that an Italian or an Irishman isn’t a
Cantonese by some legal fiction, or that everything
isn’t legally something else.
“Envy,” once wrote Emerson, “is sui-
cide.” Judge Williams, in writing to the Argus
his already historical letter laying claim to the
Victorian Chief Justiceship and denouncing Dr.
Madden, who has now been sworn in to that
office, as a poor lawyer who can only have been
chosen because he is a good advocate, has practi-
cally committed self-destruction. Had Judge
Williams’ friends not been so cock-sure, he might
have gained the object of his ambition, but Pre-
miers love surprises. The best proof of Mr.
Williams’ utter incompetency to follow Higin-
botham is shown by the letter in question, wherein,
among other foolish things, he says that for 12
years he has been doing more than his duty, but,
in view of the ingratitude with which he has been
treated, he means in future to discharge only the
letter of his judicial obligations : in connection
with which one remembers that he has had within
the period named two English holiday trips; also,
that no public functionary of the highest rank can
possibly do more than his duty—it is his duty to
give his best “not calculated less or more.” The
Bulletin questions much whether the declaration
made in Mr. Williams’ letter does not afford good
grounds for demanding his resignation. Judge
Williams is an hysterical person and has made
himself ridiculous by a long course of public
snivelling over “sad” cases, alternated by the
imposition of cruel and useless flogging sentences.
He may or may not be a better lawyer than his
successful rival, Dr. Madden, but the latter may
at least be depended on to keep a level head.
It is one of the delusions which Parkes
dishes up as their natural food to the constantly-
decreasing number of his dupes and fools that he
is “ a constitutional authority ” —though since the
first diplomatist circulated his first lie and since
the first politician invented the first herring to
draw across the trail which the people should have
followed to have gained hold of their rights, no
living soul has been able to say definitely what is
a constitutional authority. Now, “as a constitu-
tional authority,” Parkes, who occasionally strikes
the bedrock of truth, lias written, so he declares
in his book, to “ a distinguished person in another
colony ” as to the impropriety of Ministers of the
Crown appointing themselves to offices of profit
such as the Agent-Generalship. Parkes’ conten-
tion briefly is—once a Minister intimates his
intention of accepting other office than Minis-
terial, in that instant he becomes Ministerially
dead; and by all law and precedent becomes dis-
qualified for further performance of Ministerial
duty. A Minister stands on a different grade
from any other person ; he cannot disassociate
himself even in thought from the ICxecntive
function of the whole Cabinet; yet it is plain
that, when Premier John Smith intimates to
colleague Bill Jones that Bill Jones can have
the Agent - Generalship when he likes, and
Bill Jones accepts, the offer is made to and
accepted by Bill Jones as a private person
and not as a Minister. But he can’t accept
as a private person, for he is a Minister ;
and, therefore, as he accepts when still holding
Ministerial office, he, ipso facto, ceases to possess
Ministerial capacity the moment he expresses his
acceptance or, what is precisely the same thing,
his intention of accepting it, and every one of his
subsequent Ministerial acts becomes consequently
invalidated. Extend the reasoning a step farther
and suppose the Minister who is offered other
office is the Premier himself, and what is the
result? The Premier ceases to have any executive
power or function; but, when the Premier becomes
Ministerially defunct it is an axiom of Parliamen-
tary procedure that his Ministry becomes defunct
with him, and the acts of the individual Ministers
succeeding his expressed intention to acceptoutside
office, are thereupon made null and void. There
is no possibility of evading this conclusion, and,
therefore, there are two, if not three, Aus-
tralian provinces which, according to the con-
stitutional authority aforesaid, are in a beautiful
old tangle. Every act done by the Munro (Vic.)
Ministry after its head had intimated his inten-
tion to accept the Agent-Generalship was invalid •
every act done by the Griffilwraith gang after its
bowelless chief had determined to shove himself on
to the Bench (at <£looo a year more than his pre-
decessor enjoyed) was ditto; and every act done
by the Dibbs Ministry after its ex-Repubhcan
chief has squared it with Sir Saul Samuel will be
the same.
The Draper’s Bill.
(For The Bulletin.)
Barker’s wife was a little, frivolous woman, with
small, regular features, fairly proportioned, and a
pair of merry eyes that lent expression to an
otherwise ordinary face. Stability of character
she utterly lacked, yet on occasions a fiery little
temper would assert itself, and sustain her resolu-
tion till the reaction sec in. A passion for pretty
dress, fostered by childish indulgence, had grown
with her years, and now proved her ruin. All
her husband’s earnings passed into a prominent
drapery establishment, and still there remained a
debit. The firm’s collector, a cheeky, dapper
little cockney, called for settlement, and, struck
at once with the apparent levity of the little
woman, saw his opportunity, and seized it.
His employers noted how regularly their account
was presented at Parker’s, and complimented the
spare Collins on his assiduity. Little by little
the bill was reduced, and finally discharged.
Meeting Parker one day. the senior partner
stopped to chat.
“ By the way, I’m sorry to have been so impor-
tunate over that little matter.”
“ What matter?” said Parker,
“Why, that account you owed us—you know,
for your wife’s millinery.”
“My wife’s millinery ! I owed you nothing,”
echoed Parker amazedly.
Oh, no, that’s all right,” quickly the senior
said, fearful of offence, and wondering inwardly if
he had the wrong man. “I must be mistaken.
Excuse me—er —good day !”
Parker went home. “ Where’s that bill ? Let’s
see how much you have run me in for.”
Oh ! it’s settled,” said Mrs. Parker, incau-
tiously.
. T°° late she saw her cue was gone, and that her
indiscretion had taken away her only safeguard-
blank, persistent denial of the existence of any
bill.
Her husband stared; then—“ls it? Well, I
want to see it.”
She tried evasion, invented excuse after excuse,
logical and otherwise, but was met only by
reiterated demand, in a harsh voice, unlike
Parker’s usual suave tones—“ Show me the bill!”
“I’ve lost it!” “Show me the bill!” “I don’t
know where it is !” “ Show me the bill!” His
persistence roused her anger. “ I won’t!”
And he seized her by the hair, brutally flung
her out of his way, and began to search the chest
of drawers.
She sat down on the bedside, and angrily tapped
the floor with her foot. The tiny tapping tattoo
was maddening to the man, turning out receipt
upon receipt. He failed, and she greeted it with
a laugh.
A happy, unhappy thought—the pockets of her
dresses in the wardrobe ! As he went towards
the spot, /a sigh from the woman told that her
anger was leaving, and the usual outburst followed.
Parker found the bill, read it—’twas for a large
amount, and was duly receipted by Collins. Then
ho turned to the weeping woman.
“ Who paid this?” No answer. “D’ye hear!
Mho paid this?’ Silence. He shook her,
“ Answer me. Is this the price of your shame?”
Only sobs. Then he sneered—“ Cheap, too ; you
do not put a high selling price on yourself.”
She stood upright. “ You coward !” She spat
the words at him ; “I was sorry till you said
that now, I tell you,” her voice quavered with
the force of her rage ; “ I tell you I glory in it!”
“ Woman” she cut him short and turned
out into the sunshine with her worst nature upper-
most, the remembrance of that cruel sneer
repressing any better sentiments. He let her go
without a word, and gradually a hard, determined
look settled down on his face. His eyebrows met
oyer his nose, and the muscles of the neck were
tight drawn as those of one who makes a mighty
effort.

Melbourne possesses the champion modest
man. His tombstone, prepared ready for his
decease, stands in a prominent position in a stone-
mason’s yard, with an inscription setting forth
the virtues of the prospective deader in letters
two inches long. Several idiosyncracies of English
point unerringly to its authorship.

MANAGER (of the Dead Goat Mining Co,): “Splendid stone-it
averages five ounces per ton.”
SHAEEHOLDER: “That’s good, how many tons did yon crush? ”
MINE MANAGER: “Tons? I crushed fourteen pounds.”
Israel Mazey, who was a seaman on the
Duke of York, the first boat to land a crew in
S.A, 60 years ago, is a living attraction at
Alberton (S.A.). Mazey is 80 years old.
It was a part of the habitual consideration
for others peculiar to the late Victorian Chief
Justice (Higinbotham), that led him to choose the
“ vacation ” period as a convenient time to die.
Higinbotham’s oratory is burnt for ever in
the memory of all who heard it in the Victorian
Assembly. How quietly, even hypocritically, he
would begin, and gradually work up till he lashed
the waves.
The S.M. Herald makes a strange blunder
in its sketch of George Higinbotham. It says
that the dead Judge and Dean Macartney were
“compeers” at Trinity College, Dublin. Now,
the Dean was born in the last year of the last
century, being now 94 years of age. He was nearly
50 when the Judge was not yet 21.
Dean Macartney, of Melbourne, 93 years
old, being asked the secret of his great age and
vigour, answered ; “ A regular life, abstemious-
ness in food and drink, and a constant round of
duties.”

Dr. Madden’s case ?

Writes a Melbourne
correspondent to The
Bulletin :—All writs
of summons, execution,
&c., used to have the
following test ; “Wit-
ness his Honor George
Higinbotham, Esquire,
Chief Justice,’’attached
to the foot of each. By
chance one of these
came under the notice
of the late Chief.
Noticing the obnoxious
“Esquire,” he scored
it out with his pen, and
gave instructions that
the word was not again
to appear. It is well-
known that the severe
work entailed by bis
task of consolidating
the Victorian statutes
was the i mined iate cause
of his death, and it
would be the literal
truth to say the work
was written with his
life’s - blood. After
finishing his ordinary
judicial duties the
Chief Justice would
remain at the Law
Courts until 10 and 11
o’clock at night, work-
ing at the statutes.
This necessitated one
of the attendants re-
maining behind to close
the ponderous doors.
The Chief heard of it
and insisted upon clos-
ing the door himself—
which it must have
taxed his strength 'to
do. These tilings are
mere trifles in them-
selves, but they are, I
think, invaluable as in-
dicating the true char-
acter of the man. His
manner was courtesy
personified. Between
the meanest official
and the most influential
personage he made absolutely no distinction.
The beatific Hodges, holding forth in Mel-
bourne Banco Court on the nobility of his late
chief and the beauty of his private life, was a par-
ticularly interesting sight. As Hodges grows
prematurely greyer he becomes more and more
like unto the angels.
Sergeant Mills, the Yictorian Premier’s
orderly, just dead, was understood to be “of good
family.” He was a sportsman, and when the
Duke of Edinburgh, Standish and Cos. went
cockfighting in Melbourne, Mills was always in
charge of the birds.
Hay Louie, the Victorian grazier who was
the other day found drowned in the crystalline
slush of the river Yarra, could, in ’BB, have drawn
a valid cheque for iHOO.OOO; yet, when he plunged
into the mud-bed, he wasn’t worth a shilling.
Boom, thy name is Bung !
A successor to Vernon’s Lord High Chan-
cellor in “lolanthe” is to bo found in Judge
Noel, of N. Queensland. A reception was given
to him lately, “one of the principal features of
which was his Honor’s illustration of the ‘ Ta-ra-
ra-boom-de-ay dance.’ ”
The people of Hindmarsh, Adelaide, elected
a working bootmaker as Mayor on Dec. 1, and, at
first, “the classes” did their best to cast ridicule
upon him. They have decided now that it was
they who came out of the contest looking foolish,
and he will in future be severely left alone.
Newnes, the London literary pirate who
has amassed a disgusting fortune out of Tit-Bits,
is said to be starting anew Gladstonian daily.
This is a mistake. The proprietor of a successful
Tory paper would be more likely to get knighted,
and Newnes was obviously cut out for a knight.
The irony of fate ! Through the influence
of Kingston, Q.C., Col. Gordon was appointed to
succeed Gen. Downes as Acting-Commandant of
S.A. Militia. One of his first acts in that capa-
city was to suspend Kingston from duty as a
militia-sergeant, because of his celebrated deli to
Baker.
W. R. Boothby, S. A. Sheriff and Return-
ing Officer, probably received his C.M.G.-ship
through being the accredited author of the “ Aus-
tralian Ballot.” The writer is reliably informed
that Boothby was not the actual originator of the
system, which was the idea of an official in his
department.

There is “a suggestion” that J. H. Angas,
M.L.C.—whose father, George Fife Angas,
founded the South Australian Land Co. ; and
helped to found S.A. itself—should be made a
baronet, like his brother land-magnate Clarke,
of Melbourne. Angas possesses the necessary
millions, is sober, and he is moderately liberal
with the overflow of his income.
One B. F. S. Baden-Powell asserts that
R. L. Stevenson, the novelist, has spent all his
money trying to satisfy a mania for road-making
in Samoa, and is so stone-broke that when Powell
lunched with him he had to bring his own pro-
visions. In fact, he hinted that Powell might as
well bring victuals for two. Apparently the
novelist lives chiefly on cocoa-nuts at present.
Judge Hartley Williams—-“Heartless”
Williams—was at first tipped for the Victorian
Chief Justiceship. He would be the most unsuit-
able man possible. A less judicial personage never
sat on the Australian Bench. He is a most dan-
gerous, two-edge-sword sort of man, never opening
his mouth but to put his foot in it. Failing
Madden, Holroyd ought to have got the position.
The late W. A. West-Erskine, M.L.C.,
the Adelaide Brokenhillionaire, has left every-
thing to his two brothers. One of them, who used
to be eternally writing letters to the Register
about prize bulls, is the hero of the oft-told yarn
about the man who asked his bank manager for
an overdraft. “ How much do you want ? ” asked
Plutus. “ Aw—aw, how much have you got?”
George Higinbotham would never purchase
a periodical ticket on the railways. If he passed
between the courts and his suburb four times a
day, he paid each time. Higinbotham once said
at a church-meeting that the Unitarian was “the
only man who could watch with serenity and con-
fidence the march of events.” And the parson-
ridden press shrieked shrilly at the firm old rock.
The late Chief Justice Higinbotham was a
groat tea-drinker and an inveterate smoker. “He
smokes like fury and drinks tea like a fish,” said
an Irish crier at the Law Courts. Every night
at his house at Brighton his man left six or eight
filled pipes on the table of his study, which the
great Radical smoked while he read his briefs or
the papers in cases to come before him as Judge
the next day.

For the last twelve
months the capitalistic press
of Australia has lost no
chance of declaring that the
Parliamentary Labour - m era -
ber movement was “only a
flash in the pan,” and that
men carried into the House
on the spring-tide of mis-
guided public opinion would
be “carried back into-their
original obscurity ” on its
ebb. Here, the wish is
plainly father to the thought,
but as this is one way of
manufacturing public
opinion, the sooner the
labour bodies take steps to
diffuse a totally antagonistic
idea the better. Half-a-
dozen years ago there were
scarcely a dozu Protec-
tionists in the N.S. Wales
Legislature; now, there are
over seventy. The move-
ment grew, partly because
the public outside did not
lose faith in Protection be-
cause of the early failure of
parliamentarians to achieve
success, and chiefly because
the Protectionist politician
declared that Protection vns
purely a Labour movement.
Now that Dibbs, Barton and
Co. have shown that their
brand of Protection —like
the policy of the Freetrade
gang—is a “ lorn’awdah,”
freedom-of-contract, down-
with - unionism movement,
the men pledged solely to
labour reform should mop
up all the votes the Ministry
have thus alienated, and,
adding them on bo the votes
previously polled, file into
the Assembly after next
general election with an
absolute majority. The
monopolists fear this—
despite the bragging of their
“organs,” and are manufac-
turing faggot-votes at present
all they know how.
Oockburn is expected
to again hoist the banner
of the Progressive Land
Tax at next S. A. general
elections.

mise. And that’s all.

According to Government Statist Hayter
(Vic.), the Amalgamated-Shearers’Union of Aus-
tralia’s last year’s revenue was ,£18,261, while its
expenditure during that period was £22,592. The
cost of management alone is stated at .£12.221, or
nearly 67 per cent., or more than two-thirds, of
the incomings.
Sir G. Republican Dibbs is making an
heroic effort to restore the financial equilibrium in

public officials—and yet he travels to Watson’s
Bay in a Government launch when he wishes to
air his exclusive self. A penny-wise-and-pound-
foolish Premier.
One item on the invoice for the Victorian
Parliamentary picnic through the mallee lands is
“Cigars, £l4B. If somebody would pass a bill
providing that nothing bub ordinary plug and
beer should be supplied at Parliamentary picnics,
the amount saved per annum would bo sufficient
to keep the army in boots.
During ’92, N.S.W. paid £813,779 in
duties on imported grog, and only .£21,099 excise
on local distillations. What a tremendous volume
of sly-grog do not these figures suggest ! Scores
of Sydney publicans are buying for 10s. a gallon
spirits which would legitimately cost them 145.,
apart altogether from the duty.
Dear Bulletin, —l read in a medical
journal the other day that leprosy was introduced
into the Hawaiian Islands in 1865 by two Chinese;
and so rapidly did it disseminate itself that, in
1881, 4500, or one-tenth of the population, were
registered lepers. Can you tell me the proportion
in 1891 ? Or where to find it ?—J.P.C.
Higinbotbam’s Education Bill has been
overlooked. He offered the denominations exactly
what they clamour for now, and they wouldn’t
take it. The majority of the clerics (like. Victorian
Education-Minister Stephen) wanted to smash
the Catholics, whom they only consolidated while
aiming a death-blow at their own isms.
It should not be forgotten that Higin-
botham wanted to abolish the Legislative Council,
and led the way in showing that the more it was
quasi-liberalised, on a pretended representative
basis, the more dangerous it became. To-day,
Parkes, G.C.M.G., is endeavouring to lay in
N.S.W. an elective death-trap for the Democratic
party.
It is estimated that 3000 Canadians living
on the border-line cross over to the United States
to their work every morning, and return at night.
A bill now before Congress proposes to prohibit
these individuals working in the States unless
they also reside there. Even this sample of
advanced Protection, however, will probably be
hailed by Parkes and the man with the eye-glass
as another evidence of the “ Freetrade reaction.”

The Broken Hill “conspirators” will
serve the term of their sentences if Minister of
Justice O’Connor is allowed to lead the Dibbs

Government.
Energetic efforts are being made to resus-
citate the Queensland “Liberal ” party, pure and
simple. Members of the present Assembly who
are expected to form the nucleus are Dickson,
Powers, Groom, Gannon, and Donaldson—Dick-
son probably being the leader, and it is expected
they will be able to “down” the “ Kinaka-
importing-meat-exporting-land-grab ” crowd.

The Russian Government is having several
ships built in Scotland for the transport of pn'itical
prisoners to Siberia via Archangel and the Frozen
Ocean. They are fitted up and honeycombed
with narrow iron cages, in each of which some
patriot will be. chained, after tin fashion of a wild
boast. Thu solid old Presbyterians take the con-
tract for these vessels as an ordinary matter of
business, just as they would take the contract for
a slave-ship or an infernal machine, and charge
nothing extra for the curse which i\; t.j on the
unholy craft.
The Queensland Ka-
naka Ministry has indig-
nantly sacked Mar, the
G vernment analyst. Rela-
tions have been strained
between Griffilwraith and
Cos. and that official for a
long time. When the
famous lie about the
“poisoned apple,” said to
have been given by a
unionist to Private O’Cal-
laghan, during the shearers’
strike, went abroad, Mar
was instructed to make the
necessary analysis. He did
so, and burst up the story
by reporting that there was
no poison whatever in the
case; it was an ordinary
attack of biliousness, with
traces of unripe fruit and
rum. The next trouble
arose v hen the garish men-
dacity went abroad about
the tanks which the union-
ists had poisoned with
strychnine; Mar, acting
under instructions, ana-
lysed samples of the water,
and not only proved that
it contained no strychnine
or other poison, but un-
kindly added some scien-
tific facts about the impos-
sibiiity of employing
strychnine in the manner
described. Then a Govern-
ment supporter, who was
endeavouring to float or
sell a gold-mine, sent
samples of stone to Mar
for assay, and that matter-
of-fact official reported that
there wasn’t a trace of
gold anywhere. His report
burst up a promising specu-
lation. It is unlucky up
North just now to have a
diseased attachment to ac-
curacy.

A Mr. Montgomery, of Sydney, writing
to London Times, characterises Sir Henry
Parkes’s political career as weak and selfish, and
declares that he has “ shamefully used his political
friendships.” “ Justitia,” a colonist of 50 years
(probably Sir Saul Samuel), has replied defending
Parkes. Nevertheless, it is true, as stated, that
Parkes has shamefully used his political friend-
ships. His financial transactions with dozens of
members of Parliament can only be defended on
the assumption that he has never been influenced
by any of the ordinary m itives of human nature.
People are aware that in some of the
Australian provinces the Governor is entitled to
import wines, &c. free of duty; but the extent to
which this privilege is av died of, in some eases, is
hardly known. In Victoria, not only liquors but
all articles of merchandise in sealed pickages are
admitted by a Protectionist Government, which
compels common, non-irnported people to h ive
their underwear scattered over a railway platform
by an officer in search of contraband goods. 'The
Vice-Regal Smuggler and his aidekongs (who are
also profilers by it) draw Protection wages and
thus live at Freetrade cost.
The Ahgus, which of late is making a
specialty of Conservative workmen, employs an
“ Old Digger ” to put a fine constitutional gloss
on the men of Eureka. The writer unearths yet
another Kiama ghost, and tries to show that the
riots were arranged by the horrible Irish who
wanted Home Rule. In fact, there was a Ponti-
fical legate keeping dark in Melbourne ready to
take up the reins of Government when the revolu-
tion was effected. Ahgus reports of 1854 are
quoted, and the speeches of eminent respectabili-
ties at “law ’n order” meetings of the time, to
prove that an erroneous significance is attached
to the rising.
Ifc was bound to come. Says the Echuca
and Moama Record (Vic.) re Higinbothaxn:—
The deceased was a painstaking and very upright Judge,
but like the present Sir Charles Lilley, ex-Ohief Justice
of Queensland, he was imbibed with republican senti-
ments, a feature which should be looked into when Chief
Justices are appointed, which would save much unplea-
santness and inconvenience in social, legal and political
circles.
According to Sir Henry Wrixon, at the
Melbourne Foreign-trade orgy, “ the Americans
are beginning to find that Protection has crippled
and hampered them in all the markets of the
world.” According to the U.S. Treasury returns,
the exports of the Republic are now ,£4,000,000
per month more than the imports. Which means
that the crippled country is taking =£48,000,000
per annum from the outside world. At this rate,
the sooner Australia is “ crippled ” the better.
The Griffil wraith Government lately wanted
to appoint a relation of the Solicitor-General’s as
“Legal adviser to the stamp office,” a position
specially created, at a salary of ,£6OO per annum,
but as Hodgkinson, who, on account of his recent
Melbourne escapade had received notice to quit,
and was therefore reckless, threatened to play up
furioso if it passed the Cabinet, the matter was
dropped. It may be remembered that the position
of “ Commissioner for Stamps ” was also created
by the Griffilwraith Government and was con-
ferred on a gentleman presumably asa reward forhis
services on the “ Kanaka Commission ” appointed
by Griffith when it paid the latter to oppose slave-
labour.
Fat is force. As far as can be judged by
present appearances, another year of the Victorian
Premiership will send the lean Shiels gently into
his grave—or into a lunatic asylum. He was an
active and vigorous-looking man when he took
office ; now he is a nerveless wreck with a vacant,
wandering eye, and restless hands, and feet that
arc never still, and he is reputed never to sleep
more than two hours at a stretch. The desperately
well-meaning man, who takes office with a view to
patting everything straight, generally dies of
worry and leaves things worse than he found
them; it is the great, fat, profane, aggressive
person, whoso hand-grasp reminds one of contact
with a dead fish, and who has no good intentions
of any kind, who lives to draw a salary for the
best part of a generation, and then won’t shift
till lie gets a pension.
Premier Dibbs’ visit to Queen Victoria,
and the cigar he. received from Males, are bring-
ing forth va’liable results in the way of depart-
mental economy. He has been overhauling the
THE NEW VICTORIAN CHIEF JUSTICE-DR. MADDEN.
stationery, and finds that the letter-paper used is
of the most lavish character, and has the royal
arms emblazoned on it regardless of expense.
“ Everything in New South Wales Civil Service
paper is on a more pronounced scale than even
the Queen or the Prince of Wales possesses, and a
long way ahead of what is commonly used by the
English nobility.” Dibbs, who is now quite an
authority on the Queen, the Prince of Wales, and
the nobility, intends to shut down on these
Parkesian frills for the future, and the depart-
mental notepaper is simply to have a crown
stamped on the corner, and the words “New
South Wales” below, in plain, black letters.

Society
The Earl of Hopetoun was prevented by
the death of the Chief Justice from going
“officially” to see the Standish and Bagot run
at Flemington on New Year’s Day ; but, though
his Ex, in the grey frock-coat and white belltopper
had to stop at home, plain John Adrian Louis
Hope, in a sac suit and flirty billycock, bounded
about the M.C.C. reserve, smoking a very prime
tveed, and shaking a very slender, loose leg m all
the enjoyment of muftied semi -incog._ You can
always tell when Gov. Hopetoun is quite in
a crowd, because then he wears a comprehensive
chatelaine, containing cigar -cutter, match-box,
pencil, toothpick, scissors, knife, sovereign-case,
corkscrew, thermometer, aneroid, and sundry
other amulets, in his vest-pocket, and, after cutting
a cigar, he drops the whole bunch against his
knee, and the clatter makes everyone look sud-
denly round with a crick in the neck.
* * *
A caricature of a four-in-hand club is the
latest folly of hard-up Melbourne. An osten-
tatious display of wealth on wheels is a sad thing
when done on a large scale, but when three or four
would - i f-they-could turn-outs are gathered to-
gether, for the edification of a sassiety which has
filed its schedule, the effect is absolutely heart-
rending.
# * *
There is a military ring down South which
jumps all the best military appointments. Its
latest capture is the Governorship of the Mel-
bourne Gaol, which is to be handed over to a
Clerk of Petty Sessions named Burrows. He was
once a tov captain in the Victorian tin soldiery.
Evans, the Inspector-General of the Penal De-
partment, is an R.N., and Couchman, Chairman
of the Public Service Board, is a common “Colo-
nial Colonel,” and that completes the circle.
Gibson, a capable officer of 35 years’ gaol service,
and now Governor of Castlemaine Gaol, is being
passed over in this deal —the penal veteran sup-
planted by the military upstart. The same ring
will make a big effort to run in “ Colonel ” lom
Price as Chief Commissioner of Police.

* * *
An “Inspector of Tea Cosies’ will pro-
bably be added to the staff of the Victorian
Health Department. “ Householder ” has written
to the Age to point out that tea-cosies are often
lined witli red material dyed with poisonous
cochineal. “It is strange,” he wrote, “ that the
Board of Health exercises no supervision over
such matters.” Sliiels will probably create the
billet and appoint himself to it in lien of the
Chief Justiceship or Agency-General. lie will
be about ripe for another job when public feeling
is stirred up upon the tea-cosy iniquity.
• * •
The latest confidence-trick. The operator,
artistically decorated with dabs of mortar on
clothes, hat, and boots, calls at a likely house
about two o’clock on Saturday and asks to see the
master, whereupon he explains that lie is working
at the house being erected up the street, and as
his employer has been detained in some way with
their pay, they are stranded for want of a couple
of shillings to train them to Sydney. The trick
has been played with great success on the llla-
warra line, a fresh suburb being tried each
Saturday.

At a Sydney registry-office. Squatter in
want of a matried couple at <£(>o per annum, to
candidate for the employment; “ Have you any
children?” “No.” “How long are you mar-
ried?” “A year or so.” “Ah, you won’t suit

I want something that won’t breed.” Verbatim.

Notwithstanding the Makin disclosures,

• • •
Up South recently a select party, con-
sisting of a Judge, his associate and several ladies,
visited the theatre. One of the gentlemen got
very merry during the interval, and beguiled the
time during the last act by fixing the “ Reserved ”
card on to the back of one of the ladies, where it
remained.
Crown-Solicitor Stuart, of Adelaide, has
been in a blue funk over the Baker-Kingston
trouble. It was his duty to prosecute, and as
both principals have been Attorneys-General and
may be again, he was in a quandary. He jumped
at the suggestion of Symon, Q.C., to effect a com-
promise of the duel trial, and felt very lachrymose
when Baker savagely bullied him because of his
complaisance.

A Wellington (M.L.) magistrate lately
charged a local rowdy 10s. or four days in gaol for
biting off a policeman’s thumb. At that rate one
could eat a whole policeman raw for about .£2O.
m • •
A miner with a broken leg was lately
brought into the Arrowtown (M.L.) hospital.
The doctor was absent, and his return was alto-
gether indefinite ; there was no other doctor avail-
able, and after some consideration the old sailor
who acted as caretaker undertook the operation him-
self. At a subsequent inquiry the ancient mariner
was asked where and how he obtained his surgical
knowledge. “I ’adn’t no surgical knowledge,”
he responded cheerfully ; “ I just giv’d the patient
’arf a glass of brandy, took another ’arf-glass my-
self, and ’ad a buck at it.” It was a great success,
as it happened, but think of the joy of the
ordinary up-country patient when some sailor or
carpenter or glazier “ has a buck at him !”
* * *
Bowen (Q.) possesses a railway-line. It
runs from Sleepy Hollow (Bowen) to Wangaratta,
49A miles, doing the entire journey in seven and
a-half hours. It only runs twice a week ; the
passenger average is nil. The entire staff consists
of two —the engineer and the station-master, who
travels with the train, and is guard, signal-man,
light porter, lamps, ticket-taker and refreshment
bartender rolled into one. When the train reaches
a station, the guard, etc., gravely dismounts, un-
locks the ticket-office, waits a few minutes, locks
up, mounts again, and hustles the train off at the
terrific rate of nearly seven miles per hour.

• * •
In the London papers which reached us
last week there appear no less than three eases of
young men who had got into trouble for severely
whaling their sweethearts. The man who whales
his wife is common enough-especially in
England, but it is indeed a prosaic country with
all the gilt rubbed off it, where a man spanks his
best girl before marriage.
* * *
It was after a recent Sydney Sunday-
school picnic, and the lady and gentlemen teachers
were having a little innocent game among them-
selves, specially invented for the occasion by some
genius of the party. This was the way of it: The
ladies formed themselves in a line, and the gentle-
men ditto, each facing his particular weakness.
Then the inventive genius aforesaid piped out m
a rich falsetto, “ Right-about turn ! Quick
march ! ” when both columns began to move from
one another with a solemn military tread, until
about 50 yards apart,, when the genius bleated
out “Charge!” Then both lines right-about-
faced, and rushed frantically into the arms of
each other. Great order was maintained ;it being
recognised that whichever “ mash ” a man
faced at the beginning of the game was the par-
ticular girl he wished to grab and hug to his
bosom when the order “Charge!” was given,
and it was a point of honour among them that
none was to jump another’s claim, but wait his
turn, which was sure to come to rum, for the
genius changed the places every time, so that
everyone would get at least one good hug from
every girl. After an hour or so at this innocent
diversion they prayed “ Lead us not into tempta-
tion,” and went home-in pairs.

A J.P. adjudicating in a N.S.W. South
Coast court, after spending an hour or two in
suction, found himself in a very happy mood on
the bench. A case came up in which an almost
unintelligible Chinkie was a witness. ihe .J.l ~
with his skin full, stood the gibberish for a time in
a somnolent way propped up on his elbow, till
finally, leaning over towards the mouthing Chow,
he touched him on the shoulder, and in a maudlin,
yet fatherly fashion, said, “Come, John, blank
you, why ‘the (hie) don’t you (hk) shpeak
English !”
# • *
Editor Bulletin,— Why do you harp so
much on Parkes’ reputed misuse of the aspirate
“h ? ” Is it not possible that the men who omit
the use of the aspirate, except for special emphasis,
are more progressive and “correct” than those
who are painfully precise about their ns.
Language, particularly pronunciation, is ever
changing, and the conventional English of the
time of Chaucer is now difficult to understand by
any but philological experts. There is excellent
reason to believe that the aspirate “ h ’ was once
conventionally used in many words in which it is
not now allowed, and vice versa. Hour,
“honest,” “heir,” are cases in point, and, if spel-
ling were as changeable as formerly, those words
would probably customarily appear in print with-
out the “ h.” The peopleof a country are the best
authorities as to the pronunciation of its language,
and as the majority of (literate) Englishmen
omit the aspirate, unless they desire to be
particularly emphatic, or to imitate the pedants,
it is probable that Parkes is right after all. If
Webster is not ridiculous in spelling “labour
without the “ u,” why laugh at Parkes for some-
times adding emphasis to “any ” by pronouncing
it “ heny ? ” —Yours, etc., S. A.R.
• • •
Walsh, the Melbourne testator, has been
a bad speculation for the Church. It spent a lot
in costs over his contested will; it lost the case
and the money ; and now the dead man’s ticket
has won the niano in a church art union, and the
hated relatives will take the instrument away in
triumph. Still, things might be worse ; on one
occasion, in Maoriland, some years ago, they were
worse. A well-to-do Canterbury selector was
dying ; a hard-drinking old man with a bad con-
science and no near relatives. When Ids end was
at hand he resolved to effect a big fire-insurance
on his uneasy soul by leaving all his money to a
neighbouring Bethel. The parson was apprised,
and though it was late and dark and wet he
dashed off through the slush to get the will drawn
up. Towards midnight ho came back with it,
muddy, tired, and triumphant, and entered the
house at a run to procure the dying man’s signa-
ture. But an enormous, damp, ill tempered dog
was in possession of the passage and wouldn’t let
him go by. He talked appealingly to the beast
for a while, and then, finding that no use, he made
a rush for the stairs, and was badly bitten and
chased outside. While he was holding forth to the
dog the old man died —in fact, it was the noise of
the scrimmage and the preacher’s shrieks that
killed him —and the Bethel has never got another
such chance from that day to this. And nobody
found out even where the dog came from ; it_wai s
a strange animal that had stepped in out of the
rain, and when its fiendish mission was accom-
plished it went away again and never returned.
* # *
Melbourne City Coroner Youl, in his early
days of baby-farming, used to feed the poor waifs
on condensed milk ; but it disagreed with them,
and the death-rate in his establishment, he says,
was 80 per cent. The diet is about as nourishing,
he considers, as strangulation at birth, and the
condensed-milk-fed infant stands about as good a
chance of growing up healthy under the treatment.

Winter’s regime—and he’s on the Herald board
again, too.
* * *
Editor Bulletin, —In your “Correspon-
dents’ ” column, 31/12/92, you exclaim; “Where
is the Australian man or woman who can write a
good, short, natural love-story?” Well, I can.
Here goes :—While staying at a N.S.W. back-
country hotel, where the coach - horses were
changed and the passengers had dinner, I found
myself unexpectedly within hearing distance of a
conversation between one of the hotel maids and
the driver of the coach which had just arrived.
She was evidently gently upbraiding him, and
this is what I heard. She (“and her voice was
low and sweet”): “Don’t you love me now.'
He; “Yes, I love you. Blast you! Didn’t I
tell you before that I loved you?” Now, that is
a love-story. I think it is “ good.” It is certainly
short, and it is perfectly natural. It is true,
besides, and I am A Scotch Austhalee.—[Next.
We want ever so many more love-stories.—Ed. B.

nounced, leave out the “P.” The clergyman is

# * *
Lloyds’ Weekly gives, “as a warning
to widows and other unsuspecting women,”
a statement of the villainies of Bruno Van
Barne Felt, a bogus count recently convicted in
London. The medium of Ids rascalities was a
sham theatrical agency, conducted in a palatially-
fnrnished office, where numerous letters from his
dupes were discovered. Van B. Felt evidently
knew lovely woman “ right down to the ground.
Here is a letter he wrote to one of his clients :
When the loans with which my ancestors saddled my
estates are cleared off, 1 shall have a beautiful chateau to
take vou to. It is on the edge of a lake, and by the side
of a mountain, with the most lovely views In Europe.
Fancy, darling, being awakened in the morning by the
birds, and seeing the sun rise over the splendid scenery,
while the soft, balmy Spanish air is wafted in from the
window filling the room with a sweet fragrance ! Oh,
darling? you can guess! I don’t like cold, foggy London,
when mv youth has been spent in sunny Italy and France.
Ah France, darling, after we are married we will visit
together and then I shall have an opportunity of showing
my little wifey that life is. worth living.
That is the sort of thing a woman likes to hear.
Pity that a man who could write such letters
should ever be permitted to go to gaol. The
average woman would rather spend al her cash
in being deceived and robbed by a humbug of the
Felt order than keep it in the bank and be con-
fronted with the grim realities of life outside
castle.s in Spain.

Rosetta Govett has loved always wisely, if
somewhat too numerously for the prejudiced
notions of straight-laced people. She has various
children belonging to several fathers. Two
children get ss. a week each, one gets Bs., a fourth,
10s., and she wanted the Bathurst, N.S.W., P.M.
to get 15s. for a fifth from an alleged father. Said
P.M. evidently didn’t want to encourage petty
jealousies amongst the children, and so issued an
order for only 7s. fid.

The best thing in Phil May’s “ Winter
Annual ” is the picture (founded on a well-known
German idea) of the lion-tamer who came home
late and slunk into the lions’ cage to escape the
fury of his wife. She discovers the unsober wretch
resting his swollen head on the neck of a king of
beasts, and screams, “You coward 1”

The S. M. Herald, reviewing a book
named “The Clever Miss Jancy,” concludes by
stating that—“ Miss Haycraft sticks bravely to
her task, and after penning some 300 pages, allows
the reader to discover that the clever Miss Jancy
is only a woman after all, capable of feeling true
love and of making a good wife for the night
man.”

The tale of a mother-in-law was told once
again in Melbourne Divorce Court the other day.
A lady wanted a divorce on the ground of deser-
tion, and explained that her husband had aban-
doned her in Melbourne and gone to Seymour,
leaving her desolate. Then the husband rose and
explained that he had a billet in Seymour, and
lived there for some time with his wife and her
mother, but at last the old lady revolted, and
announced that she wanted to live in the
city. He wouldn’t give up his situation, so
they both left him. Whereupon, Judge
Williams denounced the iron-jawed relative as a
mischief-maker and a meddlesome old woman,
and told the husband that he ought to suppress
her and be master in his own house. The Bulle-
tin assumes that Judge Williams hasn’t got any
mother-in-law.

The various reports as to Miss Kate
Marsden’s hobnobbing with Queen Victoria and
the crowned heads of Europe, and the considera-
tion which her “ colonial experiences ” secured
her, caues endless amusement in Maoriland. The

Blue Bush,’ a Northern correspondent,
to I HE Bulletin 'The death by thirst of David
IVL Kay and his two blackboys is reported as
having taken place between Corella and Alroy.
Dave M Kay was the first white man to take cattle
and look after them on Barkly tableland, and,
in the writers knowledge, there was no better
stockman (and few better bushmen), except per-
haps “ Old Man Read ford,” on the tableland.
* e o V Vi®’ the “ Corella Lagoon ” and making across
e r ?7i (^ r * Kdward Lamb’s station on the
1 Jayford), he must have expected to hit a water-
hole on the Playford which turned out to be dry ;
then he knew all was up. His two boys, only
little fellows, but as cool and game as their master,
no doubt met their fate as quietly as he.

Last Thursday morning there was thunder
an d the crash of imprecations in
Me lb. Age. A half-crazed editor rushed round
seeking the heart’s blood of the chief of the staff
while Boss Syme awaited the arrival of Premier
Shiels’ head on a charger. “ The cause of all this
excitement was not far to seek.” The Argus, an
obscure twopenny rag, published a little higher up
the street, had got in front of the Age with the
news that Matthew Davies and Co. were to be
prosecuted by the Crown. Of all ghastly out-
rages ever perpetrated in the local newspaper
world this was the most horrible and unaccount-
able. To think of David Syme’s own (Ministry,
the Cabinet he had created and nursed through a
dozen want of confidence ’ motions, throwing
such a tit-bit of intelligence to the enemy !
Later in the day, so goes the perjury, Shiels
was allowed to call personally upon the pro-
prietor of his soul and make terms of peace.
He undertook to invent a new political
or social sensation as soon as possible, in
order that the Age’s own special reporter might
take out patent rights in the same, and have the
latest particulars all to himself. Meanwhile,
Shiels bogged Mr. Syme to accept of the informa-
tion that the Crown was about to offer, subject to
Mr. Syme’s approval, the billet of Chief justice
to Dr. Madden. Having taken a solemn oath
that the Argus would be kept ignorant of this
new item, and having again protested his regret
that there had been a leakage in the Mercantile
Bank secret, Shiels was permitted to withdraw
(backwards) from the presence of King David.
Next morning the penny oracle proclaimed the
“ Nomination of Dr. Madden ”in bold type, while
the Argus stood out in the cold and said “ Dam!”

Life in the Bush.
(For The Bulletin.)
I live in the bush, and I know the bush. One of
the many afflictions which I bear with as much
fortitude as is possible for one who is, after all,
but human, is the nightly visitation of a large
number of oxen of inquiring character and assorted
sexes. My hut is not fenced in, because if a bush
cattle means business he doesn’t trouble his head
about fences. Fence —why a coffer-dam wouldn’t
stop pur cows ! So, about the time I have got
into my bunk, and am knocking the ashes out of
niy last pipe, some robust young steer out-
side begins to paw up dirt. This is pre-
paratory to organising a party to prospect for
soap, or shirts, or towels. (Cattle are very fond
of shirts—Crimean, for choice.) Pretty soon his
comrades, and the milky mothers of the herd, and
the little cowlets, and a lot of working bullocks,
chip in. They eat up any soap that is left lying
about, also saddlecloths and undershirts and socks.
1 hen. they “moo.” Singly at first, then as
duettists, afterwards in trios, and finally as a full
orchestra. Sometimes they go away after this
but as a rule they stay till daylight. I arose one
night in my wrath, and hunted them all away
about two miles and a distance. At least, I
thought so, but when I returned, I found I had
overlooked two brindled steers and their ms.
They were inside the hut, chewing my blankets,
what is the use of telling a man he shouldn’t
swear ?
The exertion of this pursuit and the subsequent
wrath did not agree with me. I took a chill, and
afterwards contracted pneumonia, as well as a
large abscess on the liver. I also stubbed my
great toe severely while pursuing the oxen, t
made myself a roomy mustard-plaster, and applied
it to some of the aching parts. Next day I threw
it out. About evening, the same old deputation
of bovines waited on me. A meditative, matronly-
looking old cow elbowed one of her sons out of the
way, and attacked the poultice. She got a fair
grip of it, and laid it well back on her tongue.
Then the poultice began to get its work in. The
female ox laid it down, sighed deeply, and retired
to look for some sourgrass. The next candidate
was a red steer that I had once helped to pull out
of a shaft. On that occasion he had put down his
head and dispersed his rescuers as soon as he
emerged. He opened the plaster out before sam-
pling it, and thereby got about five ounces of
mustard well under his tongue before his suspicions
were aroused. Then he went away to the creek,
breathing hard. My spirits rose on seeing two vic-
tories scored in such rapid succession, and I began
to think of patenting the invention and becoming
A BAD SPEC.
HE: "Yus, I am in several mining specu-
lations at Broken Hill.”

wealthy. I was quite exhilarated. For the first
time m thirteen months I smiled. The thought
that 1 had at last got a lead on those piratical
oxen had a truly wonderful effect. For fully ten
minutes I was happy. Then one of my most
constant interviewers came along. His name is
(strawberry, and he is a working bullock. “Work-
ing ' is a courtesy title given to Strawberry
because he is yoked up with a team, but it has no
real significance, for Strawberry has long since
given up the habit of pulling. He is a gaunt
beast, and his hide is scored with old whip-marks,
so that it looks like a railway-map. His favourite
food ,s gunny-bags, but he will also eat tarpaulins
and bridles. Strawberry ambled up to the
poultice, looking modestly confident, and I realised
that my imaginings had been vain. They were
He swallowed that poultice without a tear. He
lingered over it as if he loved it,-and I saw him
nVh V / n ff tle 01 5 d , 0f T thafc mustard-plaster a fort-
night afterwards ! I mean next to try the effect
of a compost of dynamite, spread on gun-cotton.
A. CHEE.

A N.S.W. country paper remarks paren-
thetically that “the new year, 1893, is fresh from
the Almighty hour-glass.” This sounds new.
In the gloaming with my 6NUN Darling.
.Bathurst (N. S. W.) is proud of being known
as “the City of the Plains.” Why? Sodom
would be much shorter, and Gomorrah wouldn’t be
much longer ; besides, it would sound ever so
much more aboriginal.

The Australian dailies publish a cable
stating that 100,000 men are out of work at Bar-
celona (Spain). Not one editor among the crowd
seems to see anything extraordinary in 100,000
men being unemployed in a city which doesn’t
contain more than 80,000 men all told. Yet, when
some British editor doesn’t know that Perth isn’t
in Tasmania the excitement among these editors
is awful.
Freetraders smoke G N U N Darlings.
More people (says a London paper) have
heard of Jarndyce v. Jarndyce than of the great
Jennens case. Nevertheless, Dickens’ famous
Chancery suit was founded on the latter. Another
claimant has been found to the enormous property
involved, which is valued at something like twelve
millions sterling. A large portion of Birmingham
is built on the estate. Lord Howe is the present
holder of a goodly portion of these riches.
G N U N Darlings take the cake.
Messrs. J. Ireland and Cos., or Ireland’s
bond and various warehouses at Newcastle
(N.S.W.), have sent their friends a very instructive
Christmas souvenir in the form of an illustrated
brochure, with pictures of Sydney Town Hall,
Anglican Cathedral and so on, and brief com-
ments about almost everything from Captain
Cook to the Zig-Zag. The book is a great deal
more reasonable than a flower-strewn Christmas-
card, and the firm’s advertisement doesn’t stick
out too far among the pictures.
At Paddington you can buy G N U N Darlings.
Some months ago The Bulletin drew
attention to an egregious mistake in a map then
just issued for the use of N.S.W. schools. It
showed a railway passing down the centre of
Cape York through an almost uninhabited
waste. Since then the map has been distributed
to the schools with the railway in question care-
fully erased. The place has been patched up as
well as possible, but the line is still plainly visible.
The department has as yet sent no purse of
sovereigns to this office as a token of gratitude for
having been saved a ridiculous blunder.
Luxury for the million, G N U N Darlings.
Dear Bulletin, —It is claimed that the
popular educational advantages of Australia are
second to none in the world. This fact was borne
in upon me with much force the other day. In a
well-known Melbourne street I encountered a
wheelwright’s sign, wherein “tire” was boldly
rendered “ tyre .” Half a mile further on I found
another wheelwright who spelt it tyer. Just
then the delivery waggon of a much-advertised
confectioner passed; on it was emblazoned
largely—“ Confectionary Cos.” Further on, over
a tailor’s door, was the inscription “ no missfits
in the window of a butcher’s shop appeared a bill
stating that “ stakes ” could be bought at per lb.;
a fruiterer’s printed card spoke of “lemon ade”
and “ sarsaparella.” This, bear in mind, was the
result of observations made in less than a mile
walk in one street! And yet, think of how much
our education owes to the use of our eyes when
abroad. —Yours, F.
Constipation, or costiveness, is one of the
most frequent results of liver complaint, and re-
quires prompt and careful attention, for if the
faecal matter becomes impacted in the bowels
dangerous effects are to be apprehended. The
justly celebrated Dr. Abernethy said that in-
attention to the action of the stomach and bowels
was the cause of ninety per cent, of our physical
ailments, and modern science has proved this
assertion correct. Care, however, requires to be
observed in the choice of remedies, for there are
numerous drastic and violent cathartics sold under
various fancy names, but Dr. Fletcher’s Pills are
used with the best advantage in such cases.
These pills are from the prescription of Dr. Bell
Fletcher, Birmingham, England, and were used
by him in his private and General Hospital
practice there for many years with the greatest
success. This, as a matter of course, proves their
reliability, and Mr. A. M. Beauchamp, Rata,
Rangitikei, N.Z., says “ I wish to inform you
of the good Fletcher’s Pills are doing here.
Everybody uses them, and speaks well of them;
and so do I, for they are the best I ever used in
my life.” They can be obtained from F. M.
Clements, Newtown, Sydney, for thirteen penny
stamps.
Pro bono public >. GNUN Darlings.
The cutlery establishment of Mr. W. J.
Baker, 3 Hunter-street, Sydney, is now one of the
finest—if not the finest—in the city. This huge
warehouse is the embodiment, in polished steel, of
the whole art of gluttony, shaving, murder,
suicide, and death by misadventure. Among the
stock are carving-knives, cattle-knives, hunting-
knives, pen knives, pocket-knives, sheath-knives,
yachting-knives, razors in several dozen varieties
—hollow-ground Bengal razors, Swedish razors
with flat blades, Safety razors which enable the
patient to shave himself automatically, scissors,
nail scissors, flower scissors, pruning scissors,
grape scissors, tailors’ scissors, barbers’ scissors,
folding pocket scissors, all sorts, shapes and
varieties of scissors, a perfect blizzard of scissors
blowing in all parts of the premises, also scissors
Repaired and sharpened, likewise many other
varieties of scissors. And, in addition to these,
the firm trades in special descriptions of knives
for carving poultry and rending the leathern
turkey asunder at the festive Xmas season, and
for tearing apart the indurated fowl, and does all
manner of repairs, and sells almost every kind of
destructive weapon beneath the sun, besides
hundreds of other ripping and tearing implements
not enumerated.
“IT FEARS NEITHER ARMY NOR
NAVY.”
Those things used to be called the wooden walls
of England. . .
The scene was the Naval Exhibition at Chelsea,
in the Summer of 1891. The speaker was a young
man, and his auditor one of those lithe, bright-
haired girls one so often sees in this island.
“And aren’t they the wooden walls of England,
now ?” she said, as a child might ask when Noah
might be expected in Liverpool with his ark.
“Not exactly,” replied her escort, tenderly;
“ they are rather out of style. Come ; I’ll show
what sorts of walls we have now,” and he led her
off in the direction of the beautiful models of the
great steel ships of war.
The young fellow was right in assuming that
this country had a big and powerful navy, but the
chances of war decrease with the preparations
made to meet it. Besides, the interests that
would be put to hazard grow constantly larger and
nations avoid fighting as long as possible.
This is a hopeful consideration, and if England
had nothing to be afraid of beyond the danger of
being attacked from abroad, we might sleep in
peace. But there is an enemy against which
neither army nor navy is of any avail. It defies
the gunboats in the Channel and the redcoats on
the shore, and kills more people than are ever
likely to fall in battle.
If we could stop the ravages of this foe we
should soon be able to surprise our distant colonies
with the arrival among them of a splendid class of
our surplus population.
We allude, of course, to disease. Not to epi-
demics of cholera or influenza, but to diseases
which are at work year in and year out, in every
season, carrying off rich and poor alike. Unques-
tionably the worst of these is the one that attacks
the digestive system, the one from which springs
the majority of ailments, which go under various
names, as, for example, rheumatism, gout,
bronchitis, consumption, the several fevers, and
others which were formerly, erroneously, supposed
to have distinct characters, and to require distinct
treatment.
Now, however, the best medical authorities
recognise these ailments as symptoms and out-
growths of indigestion and dyspepsia, and treat
them accordingly. In illustration of what can be
done, we cite a single case. A man named
Edward Kelly, who resides at 27, St. Vincent
Street, London Road, Liverpool, having pre-
viously had perfect health, experienced a dull
pain in the right side, a bad taste in the mouth,
furred tongue, loss of appetite, discoloured skin,
unnatural langour and fatigue, and what he des-
cribes as a “ sinking feeling,’’ as though the sup-
porting power were exhausted beneath him.
This was in 1887, and he bore it without obtain-
ing relief from the usual medical treatment until
April, 1890, when one day, when he was working
in a bonded warehouse, he says, “ a dreadful pain
struck me in the back, and I had great trouble in
getting through my work. Getting worse,” he
continues, “I went to a doctor, who said it was
inflammation of the kidneys. He gave me
medicine and attended me off and on for six
months, but with no beneficial result. He said he
could not understand how I could keep on with
my work. Still, I did struggle on, though the
disease was wearing me out. From a strong, able
man, I became thin and weak, and was afraid I
should have to give up my work. Last July,
1890, a Custom House officer recommended me to
try an advertised preparation, entitled Mother
Seigel’s Syrup. I did so, and before I had
finished the first bottle the pain left my back, and
I began to digest my food and gain strength. By
continuing to use this remedy I was soon as well
as ever in my life. My master, seeing what the
Syrup had done for me, also took it for indiges-
tion, with so much benefit that now he always
keeps it by him. I have no interest whatever in
testifying thus, and only speak of the medicine as I
found it.”
Mr. Kelly evidently had a narrow escape from
Bright’s disease, a malady very common among
all classes in England, and one of the surest and
most direct products of torpid liver, itself a
symptom of indigestion and dyspepsia. We
mention this case not to put money in anybody’s
pocket, but for the sake of the sufferers who need
help—no matter what it comes from.
He Didn’t Anticipate Trouble.
AN amorous couple on the verge of committing
matrimony entered a Paddington ’bus the
other night, and oblivious of the presence of a
third passenger, who to all appearance was enjoy-
ing a comfortable nap in the top corner,
commenced billing and cooing in dead earnest.
Of course the conversation turned upon the sub-
ject of their approaching nuptials, and many were
the expressed anticipations of the joy that awaited
them in that estate which all are anxious to enter
into and not a few would be glad to obtain release
from. “ Ah,” whispered the fair one, suppressing
a mild, hysterical sob, “ what a pleasure it will be
for me to listen to all your troubles and share all
your griefs.” “But, darling,” rejoined the hope-
ful Romeo, “ I have no griefs nor troubles.”
“Oh, but you will have plenty, you know, when
we are married,” added she, with ingenuous
emphasis. The passenger in the corner coughed,
and a silence followed that would have tipped
down the scale at 300 pounds. The prospective
benedict assumed a thoughtful mood. Up to that
moment he had regarded marriage as an unmixed
blessing—a path strewn with roses—not even
once pausing to think of the discomfort attending
a pair of feminine cold feet, of an icy reception
after a late night at the lodge or club, of a colicky
baby with leathern lungs and a voice like the
shrillest of steam whistles, or of bruised and
lacerated fingers, the combined result of unskil-
fulness, a blunt tomahawk, and a chunk of knotty
firewood. These, however, are among the little
troubles that the average young man undertaking
one of the great responsibilities of life must look
out for, and the best thing for him to do is to
soothe the self-inflicted injuries of inexperience,
and to prevent the attack of chill or rheumatism
which shooting by night at lightning speed out
of a warm bed, and rushing wildly along linoleum-
covered passages in quest of paregoric will in-
evitably bring, is always to have in the house
ready for use a supply of St. Jacobs Oil, the great
conqueror of all pain. Mr. John Breakwell, of
Milson’s Point-road, North Sydney, gratefully
acknowledges; “I have suffered severely for
some years from rheumatism in my knees, and
have tried many remedies. I have found St.
Jacobs Oil gives me great relief, and am pleased
to speak in praise of it.”

published in each case before the mistake was dis-

Australian Investment Co.’s London debenture troubles
was printed in Melbourne a day before it saw the light in

The inevitable calling-up of the unpaid Anglo-
Australian I. F. and L. Co.’s unpaid capital will mean
much misery to many Sydney citizens. The shareholders’
and depositors’ meeting to consider the method of wind-
ing-up is fixed for 17th inst.
* * •
Even the strongest banks in Brisbane are calling
up advances, fearing the financial crisis, which cannot be
far off, owing to the persistent heavy fall in the shares of
some of the local institutions.

Fitzgibbon, Me ill-fated loan-floater, has returned
to Melbourne with his tail considerably down. They are
all wondering why he didn’t save expenses by staying in
Oollins-street and borrowing the money from Marks
Hermann or the representatives of Waxman deceased, or
David Henry, Esq.

W. M. K. Yale, an ex-Attorney General of
Victoria, believes that 10 members of Parliament can
always be found to justify a rogue big enough and bold
enough to embezzle £1,000,000. Ten members only’
Vale ought to know better. Just look round! Whole
Parliaments stand ready to suck the perspiration that
oozes from the feet of a millionaire thief.

A medico tells us he has tested “Storite ” in a
smal. room, hermetically scaled, and discovered no ill
effects to the head, lungs, or eyes from the smoke. We
are glad to hear it, but think that a greater test would be
m a hot rise or crosscut, where one candle burns, but two
will go out. It is in places like these, of which there are
too many in Australian mines, that miners are slowly
done to death by fumes of explosives, all of which, so far
as our experience goes, are more or less poisonous.

The Great Extended Hustler’s is again to the
front - its last yield 783 ozs. from 1000 tons. Twenty
years ago this was a long way ahead of all other Bendigo
mines, and for many months its fortnightly cakes of
between 3000 and 4000 oz. attracted thousands to the
bank-window, into and out of which they were lifted to
J n y rr - i rl ? ultitude - Then came the
n,. d »ni f l y Uußed cakes, also weighing thousands of
ozs., and for a time there was an exciting gold-cake race
ar ! d !? uc .i "’agering between these two mines. Eventu-
aliy the Ilustler s receded into hundreds and was lost to
fame. The G.G.U. continued for years, yielding to share-
holders some tons of gold, and like its one-time great
rival is again coming into the field. The shares in those
days went from pence to pounds.

Editor Bulletin,— Re your recent paragraph
concerning the affairs of the Lode Hill Tin Mmin» Cos
Are you aware that a contract was filed with” the
Registrar by the Lode Hill company’s solicitor which
contract was accepted in good faith by the vendors’ of the
property, who therefore never dreamt that their fully
pan! shares were unprotected against calls and the rapacity
of official liquidators? Now, because of a trifling in-
formality the official liquidator, with apaltry debt to liqui-
date, may legally endeavou r to “ equalise con t ribu tories ” as
it is humorously termed, by calling up some £l2 000 from
the shareholders. Why, the commission on this little
deai (if it comes off) will probably far exceed the amount
of original indebtedness ! Happy official liquidators who
thus fatten and flourish where hundreds famish and fall
headlong into insolvency (bringing, thereby, still more
grist along to the omnipotent 0.L.). How much longer
will New South Wales endure this sort of thing ?—H

* * #
The revolving stamp, probably the greatest im-
provement ever made to the ordinary crushing battery
was invented some 25 years ago—by whom, it was never
proved. A big effort to secure a patent for it was made
h y a Bendigo engineer, but his claim was long contested
and finally lost by the use of the revolving head in
America ; but whether it was used there before or after
this man conceived the idea was never actually deter-
mined. Anyhow, somebody missed a million.
# * #
George Tjansell, of Bendigo, has commenced to
erect a 100-head battery to treat quartz from his mines
on the Red, White and Blue line. The foundation stone
was laid a week or two ago by his son, a stripling of 12
years, arrayed in fine linen and a belltopper. This, we
believe, will be the flr.-t instance where a 100-head mill
has been put up on one place straight away. Roberta
and Sons, than whom there are no better battery-makers
in the world, have the contract.
• # *
The committee of investigation appointed by
depositors in the English and Australian Mortgage Bank
has reported on the ruins of that institution. The report
discourages the idea of reconstructing the bank for the
reason that there is so little bank left to build upon, and
because reconstruction would be much about the same as
fixing anew iroiclad to a foot of old keel. Yet the late
directors of E. and A.M. Bank had the cold-blocchd
impudence to draw up a list of “advantages” to be
derived by depositors and shareholders from a proposed
scheme of collecting more money and resuming the same
old game!

Justice Hartley Williams’ late father, the
old Victorian Judge, is being hauled out and dis-
cussed. He was a cushiony Methusaleh, like Lord
Snugasabuginarug, in the fairy tale. If his son,
Hartley, goes on worrying, he won’t last out as
long. Williams, sen., toyed with a knighthood
for his last year or two.
